Fifth Rak'ah with Prostration: Fard Invalidates, Transforms to Nafl
Ruling on fifth rak'ah tied by prostration in fard prayer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a fard prayer when a fifth rak'ah is completed with prostration, and explain the required remedy of adding a sixth rak'ah.

Core Idea
Adding a fifth rak'ah with prostration invalidates the fard but saves the prayer as nafl; add a sixth rak'ah to avoid odd number.

In Hanafi fiqh, when a person in fard prayer inadvertently adds a fifth rak'ah and performs its prostration, the original fard becomes invalid. However, due to istihsan, the prayer is not wasted but transforms into a nafl prayer. This passage explains the ruling and the recommended action: adding a sixth rak'ah so that the total number of rak'ahs is even, which is preferable for nafl prayers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is adding a fifth rak'ah in a fard prayer and completing it with prostration. The ruling: the fard becomes invalid, and the prayer becomes nafl.
2
Understand the condition of 'tied by prostration' (qayyada bi-sajdah)
A rak'ah is considered complete only when its prostration is performed. If the fifth rak'ah is not tied with sajda, the prayer can still be corrected.
This distinguishes between standing up for a fifth rak'ah without sujud (still correctable) versus completing it.
3
Determine the consequence: transformation to nafl
Because the final sitting (a rukn) was missed for the original fard, the fard is invalid. However, the prayer is not nullified entirely but continues as nafl.
The basis is istihsan: the forgetful worshiper's prayer is not wasted.
4
Identify the required action
The worshiper must add a sixth rak'ah to make the total even (six rak'ahs of nafl). Then he performs taslim.
Avoiding an odd number (five) for nafl is preferred.
5
Understand the reasoning
The final sitting in nafl can be delayed, so the prayer can be completed as nafl. The evidence is an athar from Hammad.
This ruling prevents the prayer from being completely void.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان قيد الخامسة بسجدة بطل فرضه وتحولت صلوته نفلا وكان عليه ان يضم اليها ركعة سادسة
[۳۰۴] (۸) اور اگر پانچویں رکعت کو سجدہ سے مقید کر دیا تو اس کا فرض باطل ہو جائے گا۔ اور اس کی فرض نماز نفل میں تبدیل ہو جائے گی اور اس پر یہ ہے کہ پانچویں رکعت کے ساتھ چھٹی رکعت ملالے۔
فرض نماز تھی اور قعدہ اخیرہ کئے بغیر پانچویں رکعت کے لئے کھڑا ہو گیا اور پانچویں رکعت کا سجدہ بھی کر لیا اس لئے پانچویں رکعت جو نفل ہے فرض کے ساتھ مل گئی اور فرض کا قعدہ اخیرہ جو فرض تھا چھوٹ گیا اس لئے نماز فاسد ہونی چاہئے لیکن نفل میں بدل جائے گی ۔ کیونکہ نفل کا قعدہ اخیرہ بعد میں کر سکتا ہے۔ اب اس کے لئے بہتر ہے کہ چھٹی رکعت ملالے تا کہ چھ رکعتیں نفل ہو جا ئیں اور پانچویں رکعت جو حقیقت میں ایک نفل ہے جونماز بتیرہ ہے اس سے بچ جائے۔ دلیل مسئلہ نمبرے میں گزر چکی ہے (مصنف عبدالرزاق نمبر ۳۴۶۱)
And if he ties the fifth [rak'ah] with a prostration, his fard becomes invalid, and his prayer transforms into nafl, and he must attach to it a sixth rak'ah. (8) And if he binds the fifth rak'ah with a prostration, then his fard becomes invalid, and his fard prayer transforms into nafl, and it is upon him to attach a sixth rak'ah to the fifth. The prayer was fard, and without performing the final sitting he stood for the fifth rak'ah and even performed the prostration of the fifth rak'ah; therefore the fifth rak'ah, which is nafl, became attached to the fard, and the final sitting of the fard, which was fard, was missed. So the prayer should become invalid, but it transforms into nafl, because in nafl prayer, the final sitting can be done later. Now it is better for him to add a sixth rak'ah so that the six rak'ahs become nafl, and he avoids the fifth rak'ah which is in fact a nafl but is an odd (watr) number, which is undesirable. The evidence has passed in the previous issue (Musannaf Abdur Razzaq no. 3461).
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage reiterates the ruling from the previous lesson but with specific emphasis on when the fifth rak'ah is 'tied' by a prostration. The author clarifies that once the fifth rak'ah is completed with prostration, the original fard prayer is invalidated because the final sitting (a fard) has been omitted. However, instead of becoming completely void, the prayer transforms into a nafl prayer. This is based on istihsan: since the worshiper's action was due to forgetfulness, the prayer is not wasted. The worshiper is then required to add a sixth rak'ah to make the total an even number (six rak'ahs as nafl), as five rak'ahs of nafl would be odd (shaf' and watr) and the general recommendation is to pray nafl in pairs. The athar from Hammad (mentioned in the previous lesson) supports this. The commentary explains that the final sitting of nafl prayer can be performed later, so the prayer is salvageable as nafl.

Key Terms
قيد الخامسة بسجدة (qayyada al-khamisah bi-sajdah): To complete the fifth rak'ah by performing its prostration, thus confirming it as a rak'ah.
نفل (nafl): Supererogatory prayer; here, the invalid fard prayer becomes nafl.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Continue with the fifth rak'ah as if nothing happened.
D) Abandon the prayer immediately and start over.

Key Takeaway
If a fifth rak'ah is added and tied with prostration in fard prayer, the fard is invalidated but the prayer continues as nafl, and a sixth rak'ah must be added.

Sitting in Fourth Then Standing Without Salam Thinking It's First Sitting
A ruling on returning to sitting after standing from the fourth rak'ah without salam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a worshiper who stands after the fourth rak'ah without salam, thinking it was the first sitting, and identify the condition that triggers returning to sitting.

Core Idea
If you stand from the fourth rak'ah before salam, return as long as you haven't fully stood.

This lesson examines a specific scenario in prayer: after completing the fourth rak'ah, a worshiper sits for the final tashahhud but then stands up without saying salam, mistakenly thinking it was the first sitting after two rak'ahs. The ruling prescribes returning to sitting, but with a critical condition. Understanding this condition helps preserve the integrity of the prayer sequence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the error
The act is the prayer after the fourth rak'ah. The error is standing up without salam due to confusion between the first and final sitting.
The worshiper intended the first sitting but actually performed the final sitting.
2
Extract the ruling
The ruling: 'He returns to the sitting' (يقعد). This is a command indicating obligation.
The Arabic verb 'عاد' (returns) implies a required corrective action.
3
Identify the condition
The condition is 'as long as he has not [fully stood]' (مالم). The text cuts off but the meaning is clear: return is only valid if he hasn't completed the standing motion.
In Hanafi fiqh, 'مالم' sets a limit for the ruling.
4
Infer the opposite case
If he has fully stood, he should not return. Instead, he completes the prayer and performs sajda sahw (prostration of forgetfulness) at the end.
This is the standard Hanafi position for similar forgetfulness cases.

Prostrating the Fifth Rak'ah Invalidates Fard Prayer
Ruling that prostration of the fifth rak'ah turns fard into nafl

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when one prostrates a fifth rak'ah after forgetting the final sitting, explain the transformation to nafl, and apply the required action of adding a sixth rak'ah.

Core Idea
Prostrating the fifth rak'ah invalidates the fard, turning it into nafl; add a sixth rak'ah to avoid odd nafl.

This lesson addresses a specific scenario in salah: a person forgets the final sitting (qadah akhirah) of a fard prayer, stands for a fifth rak'ah, and performs its prostration. The ruling is not simply that the prayer becomes invalid; rather, it transforms into a nafl prayer, requiring a sixth rak'ah to complete. Understanding this transformation is key to applying the correct remedy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the omission
Determine that the person omitted the final sitting (wajib) and added a fifth rak'ah with prostration.
2
Apply the ruling of invalidation and transformation
State that the fard is invalidated (batil) but the prayer transforms into nafl because prostration confirms the fifth rak'ah.
Hanafi ruling: the fifth rak'ah becomes nafl and the whole prayer becomes nafl.
3
Derive the required action
The person must add a sixth rak'ah to complete a six-rak'ah nafl, avoiding an odd number (batira).
4
Note the dalil
The proof is a mursal hadith from Hammad (Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq) instructing to add a sixth, indicating the fifth is considered nafl.
The hadith is zanni in thubut and dalala.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۳۰۴] (۸) وان قيد الخامسة بسجدة بطل فرضه وتحولت صلوته نفلا وكان عليه ان يضم اليها ركعة سادسة [۳۰۵] (۹) اور اگر پانچویں رکعت کو سجدہ سے مقید کر دیا تو اس کا فرض باطل ہو جائے گا۔ اور اس کی فرض نماز نفل میں تبدیل ہو جائے گی اور اس پر یہ ہے کہ پانچویں رکعت کے ساتھ چھٹی رکعت ملالے۔ فرض نماز تھی اور قعدہ اخیرہ کئے بغیر پانچویں رکعت کے لئے کھڑا ہو گیا اور پانچویں رکعت کا سجدہ بھی کر لیا اس لئے پانچویں رکعت جو نفل ہے فرض کے ساتھ مل گئی اور فرض کا قعدہ اخیرہ جو فرض تھا چھوٹ گیا اس لئے نماز فاسد ہونی چاہئے لیکن نفل میں بدل جائے گی ۔ کیونکہ نفل کا قعدہ اخیرہ بعد میں کر سکتا ہے۔ اب اس کے لئے بہتر ہے کہ چھٹی رکعت ملالے تا کہ چھ رکعتیں نفل ہو جا ئیں اور پانچویں رکعت جو حقیقت میں ایک نفل ہے جونماز بتیرہ ہے اس سے بچ جائے۔ دلیل مسئلہ نمبرے میں گزر چکی ہے (مصنف عبدالرزاق نمبر ۳۴۶۱)
[304] (8) And if he ties the fifth with a prostration, his fard is invalidated and his prayer turns into nafl, and he must add to it a sixth rak'ah. [305] (9) [Urdu: And if he restricts the fifth rak'ah with a prostration, his fard becomes invalid. And his fard prayer will be transformed into nafl, and he must add a sixth rak'ah to the fifth. It was a fard prayer, and he stood for the fifth without the final sitting, and he also prostrated the fifth rak'ah. Therefore, the fifth rak'ah, which is nafl, joined the fard, and the fard's final sitting, which was fard, was missed. So the prayer should be invalid, but it will turn into nafl because the nafl's final sitting can be done later. Now it is better for him to add a sixth rak'ah so that six rak'ahs become nafl, and he avoids the odd-numbered nafl (batira). Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of forgetting the final sitting. It presents the ruling when a person not only stands for the fifth rak'ah but also performs the prostration of that fifth rak'ah. In this case, the fard prayer becomes invalid (batil) and transforms into a nafl (supererogatory) prayer. The reason is that by prostrating, the fifth rak'ah becomes established and cannot be cancelled. Since the final sitting was omitted, the fard is lost, but because nafl prayer does not require a final sitting at that specific point (it can be done later), the prayer becomes nafl. To avoid an odd number of nafl rak'ahs (which is disliked as 'batira'), the person must add a sixth rak'ah, making it a six-rak'ah nafl prayer. The proof is the same athar from Hammad cited in the earlier case.

Key Terms
بتیرہ (batirah): An odd-numbered nafl prayer (e.g., single rak'ah) which is disliked (makruh) because the Prophet forbade praying witr with one rak'ah only? Here it refers to a five-rak'ah nafl being odd and thus disliked.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway

Returning to the Final Sitting After Mistakenly Standing
Ruling with proof: final sitting mistaken for first

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a person who stands up after the final sitting mistakenly thinking it is the first sitting, and explain the required action and its proof.

Core Idea
If you stand after the final sitting thinking it's the first, return before prostrating the fifth rak'ah and perform sajda sahw.

Have you ever lost count in prayer and mistakenly stood up after what you thought was the first sitting, but it was actually the final one? The Hanafi school provides a clear ruling for this common forgetfulness scenario. This lesson walks you through the remedy: returning to the sitting before the next prostration, completing the prayer with salam, and performing sajda sahw to compensate for the mistake.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The case: a person sits in the fourth rak'ah (final sitting), then stands up without saying salam, mistakenly thinking this sitting was the first sitting (qa'da ula). He has already performed the final sitting correctly.
The final sitting is complete; the fard (obligatory) act of sitting has been fulfilled. Only the wajib (necessary) act of salam remains.
2
Assess the stage of the mistake
The ruling depends on whether the person has prostrated for a fifth rak'ah. If he has not yet prostrated, he may return to the sitting. If he has prostrated, the fifth rak'ah becomes established and another rule applies (turning the prayer to nafl).
This distinction is critical: the boundary is the prostration of the fifth, not the act of standing.
3
Apply the ruling for the case before prostration
If he has not prostrated the fifth, he must return to the sitting (qa'da), recite tashahhud, say salam, and then perform sajda sahw. The prayer becomes complete because the final sitting was already performed correctly.
The compensation (sajda sahw) is required because he added an extra standing (qiyam) unnecessarily.
4
Understand the proof (dalil)
The ruling is based on a hadith (recorded by Dar Qutni) indicating that if one is close to completing the standing, he should not sit but perform sajda sahw; conversely, if he can still return, he should. This hadith supports the return before the prostration.
The hadith is not quoted verbatim but is referenced as evidence for the principle of returning to the proper position before completion of the erroneous act.
5
Recognize the Hanafi position and its basis
The Hanafi school holds that the final sitting is a fard (obligatory) act? Actually, in Hanafi fiqh, the final sitting is a fard (rukn) but the Salam is wajib. However, here the text states that the fard is complete because the final sitting was done; only Salam remains which is wajib. The return is wajib to perform Salam. Sajda sahw compensates for the delay and extra standing.
The cross-madhhab difference: Some other schools may not require returning if the person intended to stand for another rak'ah, but Hanafi requires it before the prostration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۳۰۵] (۹) وان قعد فى الرابعة ثم قام ولم يسلم يظنها القعدة الاولى عاد الى القعود مالم ص ۲۵۵ نمبر ۱۰۳۶ار دار قطنی ، باب الرجوع الی القعود قبل استتمام القیام ج اول ص ۳۶۷ نمبر ۱۴۰۳) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ کھڑے ہونے کے قریب ہو تو نہ بیٹھے اور سجدہ سہو کرے۔ (۹) اور اگر چوتھی رکعت میں بیٹھا پھر کھڑا ہوا اور سلام نہیں پھیرا ، اس نے اس کو گمان کیا کہ یہ قعدہ اولی ہے تو لوٹے گا قعدہ کی طرف جب تک پانچویں رکعت کا سجدہ نہ کرے اور سلام کرے اور سجدہ سہو کرے۔ قعدہ اخیرہ کر چکا ہے اس لئے فرض تو مکمل ہو گیا ہے اب صرف سلام باقی ہے جو واجب ہے۔ اس لئے پانچویں رکعت کا سجدہ کرنے سے پہلے پہلے قعدہ کی طرف لوٹ آئے اور سلام کر کے سجدہ سہو کرے پھر تشہد پڑھ کر سلام پھیرے۔ چونکہ پانچویں رکعت کے سجدہ سے پہلے ہے اس لئے ایک رکعت مکمل نہیں ہوئی اس لئے اس کو چھوڑ سکتا ہے۔ ادھر چار رکعت فرض مکمل ہو جائے گی۔ [۳۰۶] (۱۰)

[Reference to hadith from Dar Qutni: ...] From this hadith, it is understood that if he is close to standing, he should not sit and should perform sajda sahw. (9) [Urdu: And if he sits in the fourth rak'ah, then stands up and does not say salam, thinking it is the first sitting, he will return to the sitting as long as he does not prostrate the fifth rak'ah, and say salam and perform sajda sahw. He has already performed the final sitting, so the fard is complete; only the salam remains, which is wajib. Therefore, before prostrating the fifth, he should return to the sitting, say salam, and perform sajda sahw, then recite tashahhud and say salam. Since it is before the prostration of the fifth, the rak'ah is not complete, so he can leave it. The four rak'ahs of fard will be completed.]
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage deals with a scenario where a person correctly sits for the final sitting (qa'da akhirah) in the fourth rak'ah but, due to forgetfulness, thinks it is the first sitting (qa'da ula) and stands back up without saying salam. The ruling is that he must return to the sitting as long as he has not prostrated the fifth rak'ah (if he stands for a fifth). The text clarifies that since the final sitting has already been performed, the fard prayer is complete; only the salam (which is wajib) remains. Therefore, he should go back to the sitting, say salam, and perform sajda sahw. If he has already prostrated the fifth rak'ah, then the fifth becomes established, and the ruling of the previous case (turning to nafl) applies. The proof is a hadith from Dar Qutni about returning to sitting before completing the standing.

Key Terms
قعدة أولى (qa'da ula): The first sitting in a prayer of three or four rak'ahs, occurring after the second rak'ah. Here the person mistakenly thinks the fourth rak'ah's sitting is the first.
قعدة أخيرة (qa'da akhira): The final sitting in a prayer, occurring after the last rak'ah before salam. In this lesson, it is correctly performed but mistaken for the first.
سجدہ سہو (sajda sahw): Prostrations of forgetfulness performed after salam to compensate for mistakes like extra standing, delay, omission of a wajib, etc.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what should a person do if he sits in the fourth rak'ah, then stands up mistakenly thinking it was the first sitting, and has not yet prostrated a fifth rak'ah?
A) Continue standing and complete a fifth rak'ah, then add a sixth.
B) Return to the sitting, say salam, and perform sajda sahw.
C) Immediately perform sajda sahw and say salam from standing.
D) Ignore the mistake and continue as if he completed four rak'ahs.

Key Takeaway
If you mistakenly stand after the final sitting before salam, return to the sitting as long as you haven't prostrated a fifth rak'ah, then complete with salam and sajda sahw.

Adding a Sixth Rak'ah After Prostrating a Fifth: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Ruling and inter-madhhab dispute on fifth rak'ah addition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for prostrating a fifth rak'ah after the final sitting and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
If you prostrate a fifth rak'ah after final sitting, add a sixth to make nafl; Shafi'i says only sajda sahw suffices.

Imagine you are praying Zuhr and mistakenly stand for a fifth rak'ah after the final sitting. What should you do? The Hanafi school provides a detailed remedy that preserves your fard and avoids the disliked odd nafl. This lesson examines the ruling and the disagreement with Imam al-Shafi'i, who relies on a hadith of the Prophet to argue the prayer is still valid with only sajda sahw. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to weigh textual evidence and school principles.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi Position
Identify what the Hanafi school says: if the final sitting was done, the fard is complete; the fifth rak'ah becomes nafl, and a sixth should be added to avoid the disliked odd nafl (batira).
2
Identify the Opponent and Their View
Recognize that Imam al-Shafi'i holds that adding a fifth rak'ah does not invalidate the fard; the prayer remains valid with only sajda sahw, no sixth needed.
3
Follow the Opponent's Proof
Examine the Shafi'i dalil: the hadith from Muslim where the Prophet prayed five rak'ahs and performed sajda sahw without adding a sixth.
4
Analyse the Hanafi Response
Understand the Hanafi critique: the hadith does not specify whether the Prophet performed the final sitting before the fifth. If he did, it aligns with Hanafi; if not, the prayer becomes nafl. The Hanafi position is precautionary.
5
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf
Identify the practical consequence: under Hanafi, if one prostrates the fifth without final sitting, the prayer becomes nafl and must be repeated as fard; under Shafi'i, it remains valid with sajda sahw.

[Urdu continues: ...until he prostrates the fifth and says salam and performs sajda sahw. He has already done the final sitting, so the fard is complete; only salam remains. Therefore, before prostrating the fifth, he returns to the sitting, says salam, and performs sajda sahw, then recites tashahhud and says salam. Since it is before the prostration of the fifth, the rak'ah is not complete, so he can leave it. The four rak'ahs of fard will be complete. (10) And if he restricts the fifth rak'ah with a prostration, he will add a sixth rak'ah to it, and his prayer will be complete. And these two rak'ahs will be nafl. Since he did the final sitting, the four rak'ahs of fard are complete. However, the fifth rak'ah is a nafl prayer of batira (odd) which is prohibited, so he adds a sixth to make two nafl rak'ahs. (2) There is an athar from Qatada regarding a man who prayed Zuhr as five: he said add a rak'ah to it, so it becomes Zuhr prayer and two rak'ahs after it as nafl. (Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq) In this athar, it appears that he sat after four, so the four Zuhr are complete and the remaining two become nafl. If the fara'id are complete, adding nafl does not invalidate the fard. Imam Shafi'i holds that if he adds a fifth rak'ah, the four rak'ahs of fard are complete. Their proof is the hadith from Abdullah: The Prophet prayed five with us... then he performed two prostrations of forgetfulness. (Muslim) This hadith does not prove whether he sat for the final sitting before standing for the fifth or not. And he performed two sajda sahw and completed the four fard. Therefore, whether he sat for the final sitting or not, he will perform sajda sahw and the four fard will be complete.]
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage contains a ruling and an inter-madhhab dispute. The ruling: if a person has already performed the final sitting (qa'da akhirah) and then stands for a fifth rak'ah and prostrates it, he must add a sixth rak'ah to make the additional two rak'ahs a pair of nafl, avoiding the disliked odd-numbered nafl (batira). The fard is considered complete because the final sitting was done. Then the text discusses the Shafi'i position: Imam Shafi'i holds that if a fifth rak'ah is added even without the final sitting, the fard remains valid and only sajda sahw is required, based on the hadith where the Prophet prayed five rak'ahs and then performed sajda sahw. The Hanafi response distinguishes that the hadith does not clarify whether the Prophet sat for the final sitting before standing for the fifth. The Hanafis maintain that if the final sitting was performed, the five-rak'ah case is similar to the Prophet's action, but if not, the prayer becomes nafl. The text concludes that in all cases, sajda sahw is performed, and the four fard are complete if the final sitting was done.

Key Terms
بتیرہ (batirah): An odd-numbered nafl prayer (e.g., one rak'ah or five) that is disliked (makruh) because it resembles the witr prayer in being odd without pairing.
قعدہ اخیرہ (qa'da akhirah): The final sitting in prayer, which includes the tashahhud and is a wajib (obligatory) act in the Hanafi school.
سجده سہو (sajda sahw): Two prostrations performed to compensate for certain forgetfulness errors in prayer, such as adding or omitting wajib acts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what should a person do after prostrating a fifth rak'ah if he had already performed the final sitting?
A) Repeat the entire prayer from the beginning.
B) Perform sajda sahw and consider the prayer complete as four rak'ahs.
C) Add a sixth rak'ah to make the extra two rak'ahs a pair of nafl.
D) Ignore the fifth rak'ah and continue as if nothing happened.

Key Takeaway
If you prostrate a fifth rak'ah after the final sitting, add a sixth to complete a pair of nafl; the fard is preserved and the disliked odd nafl is avoided.

Doubt in Number of Rak'ahs: First Time
Ruling on first-time doubt in prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a first-time doubter between three and four rak'ahs and identify the proof from the companion's statement.

Core Idea
First-time doubt in prayer requires restarting; only habitual doubt is excused.

Doubt (shakk) during prayer about the number of rak'ahs is a common concern. The Hanafi school distinguishes between a person who experiences doubt for the first time and one who is frequently afflicted (waswasi). This lesson examines the ruling for the first-time doubter, based on a companion's directive.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and ruling
The passage describes a person who doubts whether they have prayed three or four rak'ahs. The ruling is that they must restart the entire prayer from the beginning.
This applies specifically to a four-rak'ah fard prayer.
2
Locate the proof
The proof is an athar from Ibn Umar: 'He repeats until he preserves (remembers).' This is a companion statement indicating the obligation to repeat.
The dalil is a qawl sahabi, which is considered a valid proof in Hanafi usul.
3
Understand the condition of 'first time'
The ruling is explicitly conditioned on this being the first occurrence of such doubt. If the person is a habitual doubter, the ruling changes.
The text says 'first time it has happened to him.'
4
Contrast with habitual doubt
For a habitual doubter, the Hanafi ruling is to ignore the doubt and build on certainty (e.g., assume the lesser number and perform sajda sahw).
This distinction prevents excessive repetition.
5
Note the dalil type and its weight
The athar is transmitted via Musannaf Ibn Abi Shayba; its thubut is zanni (probable transmission), and its dalala is zanni (the command 'يعيد' indicates obligation).
Hanafis accept qawl sahabi as a source of law in specific cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۱۳۰۷ (۱۱) جس کو شک ہو گیا نماز میں ، پس نہیں جانتا ہے کہ تین رکعت پڑھی یا چار رکعت اور یہ پہلی مرتبہ اس کو پیش آیا ہے تو شروع سے نماز پڑھے گا۔ اثر میں ہے عن ابن عمر في الذى لايدرى ثلاثا صلى او اربعا قال يعيد حتى يحفظ (ب) (مصنف ابن ابی شبیة)
(11) Whoever doubts in his prayer, and does not know whether he prayed three or four rak'ahs, and this is the first time it has happened to him, he will pray the prayer from the beginning. There is an athar from Ibn Umar regarding one who does not know whether he prayed three or four: he said, 'He repeats until he preserves (remembers).' (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shayba)
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi ruling for a person who experiences doubt (shakk) in the number of rak'ahs, specifically between three and four, during a four-rak'ah fard prayer. The ruling is conditional: if this is the first time such doubt has occurred, the person must restart the entire prayer from the beginning. The proof is an athar from Ibn Umar (a companion) who instructed repeating the prayer until one can remember. The text implies that for a person who frequently experiences doubt, a different ruling applies (such as building on certainty). The ruling is based on the principle that one should not proceed with uncertainty unless it becomes habitual.

Key Terms
شک (shakk): Doubt or uncertainty about the number of rak'ahs performed in prayer. Here it specifically refers to uncertainty between three and four rak'ahs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Assume he prayed three and add one more rak'ah, then perform sajda sahw.
B) Assume he prayed four and say salam without action.
C) Restart the entire prayer from the beginning.
D) Continue the prayer and perform sajda sahw at the end.

Key Takeaway
For a first-time doubter in prayer, the safest course is to restart the prayer, based on the companion's instruction.

Rectifying an Extra (Fifth) Rak'ah in Fard Prayer: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Ruling on adding a fifth rak'ah and the Shafi'i dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling for rectifying an extra rak'ah in fard prayer and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
Adding a fifth rak'ah does not invalidate fard if rectified: return if no sajdah, add a sixth if sajdah performed.

This passage addresses a common scenario in salah: forgetfully adding a rak'ah. The Hanafi school provides a clear method to rectify without invalidating the prayer, but the Shafi'i school disagrees, relying on a practice of the Prophet (peace be upon him). Understanding this dispute helps you apply the correct ruling in practice and strengthens your command of dalil-based reasoning.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
What exactly does the Hanafi school rule for a person who adds a fifth rak'ah in a four-rak'ah fard prayer? Identify the two scenarios based on whether the sajdah of the fifth has been performed.
Key: the fard is already complete due to the final sitting.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Which school objects and on what grounds? State the Shafi'i view that the fifth rak'ah itself completes the fard.
Imam al-Shafi'i is the opponent.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
What is the Shafi'i dalil? Examine the hadith of Ibn Mas'ud where the Prophet prayed five rak'ahs and performed sajda sahw.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
How does the Hanafi school rebut this hadith? The key point is whether the Prophet had performed the final sitting after four before standing for the fifth; the hadith is ambiguous.
The Hanafi response relies on the ambiguity to prefer the athar of Qatada.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
What is the practical difference in rulings? For the Hanafi, if the sajdah is already performed, a sixth rak'ah must be added; for the Shafi'i, the fifth alone suffices.
This affects whether the extra rak'ah becomes a single nafal or a pair.

He prostrates for the fifth and makes salam and performs sajda sahw. (10) And if he tied the fifth with a prostration, he adds another rak'ah to it, and his prayer is complete, and the two rak'ahs are supererogatory. (Urdu explanation: If someone forgetfully adds a fifth rak'ah, if he has not yet performed the sajdah of the fifth, he should return to the sitting, make salam, and perform sajda sahw. Since he has done the final sitting, his fard is complete, only salam remains, which is wajib. So before the sajdah of the fifth, he returns to the sitting and makes salam and does sajda sahw, then recites tashahhud and makes salam. Since it is before the sajdah of the fifth, one rak'ah is not completed, so he can leave it. In this way the four fard rak'ahs become complete. [306 (10) And if he tied the fifth rak'ah with a prostration, then he adds a sixth rak'ah to it, and his prayer becomes complete, and these two rak'ahs become supererogatory. Since he has completed the final sitting, the four fard rak'ahs become complete. However, the fifth rak'ah is a disliked supererogatory prayer (batra'), which is prohibited, so he adds a sixth rak'ah so that it becomes two supererogatory rak'ahs. (2) There is an athar from Qatada concerning a man who prayed Zuhr five rak'ahs: he said: 'He adds one rak'ah to it, so it becomes Zuhr prayer with two rak'ahs after it as supererogatory.' (Musannaf Abdurrazzaq, chapter on a man praying Zuhr or Asr five, vol.2 p.1303 no.3460) In this athar, it is as if he sat after four rak'ahs, so four rak'ahs of Zuhr are complete, and the remaining two rak'ahs become supererogatory. When the fara'id are complete and then supererogatory are added, the fard is not invalidated. According to Imam Shafi'i, if one adds a fifth rak'ah, the four fard rak'ahs become complete. Their proof is this hadith: 'Abdullah said: The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) prayed with us five rak'ahs. We said: O Messenger of Allah, has something been added to the prayer? He said: What is that? They said: You prayed five. He said: I am a human being like you; I remember as you remember and forget as you forget. Then he performed two prostrations of forgetfulness.' (Muslim, chapter on who prays five etc., p.213 no.572; Abu Dawud, chapter on one who prays five, p.153 no.1019) This hadith does not prove whether he sat for the final tashahhud after four rak'ahs before standing for the fifth, or stood without final sitting. And he performed two sajda sahw and thus completed four fard rak'ahs. Therefore, whether he sat for final sitting or not, in all cases he should perform sajda sahw, and then the four fard rak'ahs become complete.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute concerning a person who mistakenly prays a fifth rak'ah in a four-rak'ah fard prayer (like Zuhr). The Hanafi position, based on an athar from Qatada, distinguishes two scenarios: (1) If the person has not yet performed the sajdah of the fifth rak'ah, he should return to the sitting, recite the tashahhud, make salam, and then perform sajda sahw. The fard is complete because he already performed the final sitting (qa‘da akhira). The fifth rak'ah is cancelled before it is completed. (2) If he has performed the sajdah of the fifth rak'ah, then he must add a sixth rak'ah to make the extra two rak'ahs a pair of supererogatory prayer, because praying a single rak'ah as nafal is makruh (batra'). The fard remains valid. The Shafi'i school holds that simply adding a fifth rak'ah completes the fard, based on the hadith of Ibn Mas'ud where the Prophet prayed five and then performed sajda sahw. The Hanafi response is that the hadith does not specify whether the Prophet had performed the final sitting after four rak'ahs before standing for the fifth. Since the possibility exists that he did not sit, the hadith is not conclusive against the Hanafi position. Therefore, the Hanafi ruling stands: the extra rak'ah must be rectified according to the two scenarios.

Key Terms
سجدة السهو (sajdat al-sahw): Two prostrations performed to compensate for forgetfulness (sahw) in prayer.
قعدة أخيرة (qā'dah akhīrah): The final sitting after the last rak'ah of the fard prayer, in which tashahhud is recited.
نافلة (nāfilah): Supererogatory (voluntary) prayer performed in addition to the fard.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what should a person do if they have already performed the sajdah of a mistakenly added fifth rak'ah in Zuhr prayer?
A) Cancel the fifth rak'ah and perform sajda sahw.
B) Add a sixth rak'ah and then perform sajda sahw.
C) Repeat the entire prayer.
D) Complete the prayer as five and perform sajda sahw.

Key Takeaway
When a fifth rak'ah is added in fard prayer, the Hanafi ruling ensures validity through either returning before the sajdah or adding a sixth rak'ah, based on the athar of Qatada.

Rules for Doubt (Shakk) in the Number of Rak'ahs
Ruling on doubt in rak'ahs: first occurrence vs frequent occurrence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule for doubt in rak'ahs: distinguish between first-time and frequent doubt, and determine whether to repeat the prayer or build on conjecture/certainty.

Core Idea
First-time doubt in prayer requires repetition; frequent doubt is resolved by predominant conjecture or certainty, with sajda sahw.

This passage presents the Hanafi ruling on doubt concerning the number of rak'ahs during prayer. It addresses two situations: when doubt occurs for the first time and when it occurs frequently. The ruling is derived from two hadiths and an athar, which the Hanafi school reconciles by applying each to a specific context.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Distinguish between shakk that occurs for the first time and shakk that occurs frequently. The ruling differs based on this distinction.
First time = never happened before; frequent = happens often.
2
State the ruling for each scenario
For first-time doubt: the prayer must be repeated from the beginning (isti'naf). For frequent doubt: if there is a predominant conjecture (zann ghalib), act on it; if no conjecture, build on certainty (yaqin) – the lesser number – and add sajda sahw.
3
Locate the proofs
The first-time ruling is based on the athar of Ibn Umar. The frequent-doubt rulings are based on two hadiths: one commands building on certainty (Abu Sa'id al-Khudri), the other commands seeking the correct amount (Ibn Mas'ud).
The commentator reconciles by applying the athar to first-time and the hadiths to frequent.
4
Understand the reconciliation
The athar commanding repetition applies only when doubt occurs for the first time. The hadiths are general but are understood to apply to frequent doubt, as the Prophet's instruction to build on certainty or seek correctness addresses the common situation of frequent doubt.
This reconciliation avoids contradiction between the texts.
5
Apply the rule to a case
Given a person who often doubts between three and four rak'ahs but has no stronger feeling either way, he should assume three (certainty), add one rak'ah, and perform sajda sahw. If he frequently doubts and is more confident it is four, he completes four and does sajda sahw.
The sajda sahw is required in both subcases of frequent doubt.

(11) One who becomes doubtful in his prayer, and he does not know whether he prayed three or four rak'ahs, and this is the first time it happened to him, then he shall begin the prayer from the beginning. There is an athar from Ibn Umar concerning one who does not know whether he prayed three or four: he said: he should repeat until he memorizes. (b) (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah, 239 footnote: (a) ... (b) ...). And it is said: 'he does not know whether three or four, and that is the first time it occurred to him, so he begins the prayer anew.' (12) But if it occurs to him frequently, he builds on his predominant conjecture if he has conjecture, and if he has no conjecture, he builds on certainty. The chapter of one who said: when in doubt and does not know how many he prayed, he repeats. We apply this athar to mean: if doubt occurs for the first time, he prays from the beginning; if doubt occurs frequently, he acts on predominant conjecture or on certainty. Lexical note: isti'naf means to begin anew. (12) And if doubt occurs to him frequently, he builds on predominant conjecture. For example, if he doubts between three and four and his predominant conjecture is that he prayed four, he acts on that and makes salam. If there is no predominant conjecture, then three is certain, so he bases on three and adds one rak'ah to make four, and also performs sajda sahw. There is proof from Abu Sa'id al-Khudri that the Prophet said: 'When one of you doubts in his prayer and does not know how many he prayed, three or four, let him cast aside the doubt and build on what he is certain of, then prostrate two prostrations before making salam.' (Muslim, Tirmidhi) This hadith indicates that when there is no predominant conjecture, one builds on certainty. Another hadith states that one can act on predominant conjecture: 'Abdullah said: The Messenger of Allah prayed... and said: When one of you doubts in his prayer, let him seek what is correct and complete it, then prostrate two prostrations.' Another hadith says: 'Let him look to what is closest to correctness.' This hadith shows that one should ponder how many rak'ahs he prayed so that he can act on predominant conjecture.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof concerning doubt (shakk) about the number of rak'ahs prayed. It distinguishes two cases: (1) First occurrence of doubt: The person must repeat the prayer from the beginning (isti'naf). This is based on the athar of Ibn Umar, which the commentator interprets as applying only to the first time such doubt occurs. (2) Frequent doubt: The person should rely on his predominant conjecture (ghalib al-zann). If he has a stronger feeling that he prayed four rak'ahs, he acts accordingly and makes salam. If he has no prevalent feeling, then he must rely on certainty (yaqin), which is the lesser number (three), and add one rak'ah to complete four, followed by sajda sahw. The proof for building on certainty when there is no conjecture is the hadith of Abu Sa'id al-Khudri: 'Cast aside doubt and build on what is certain.' The proof for using predominant conjecture is the hadith of Ibn Mas'ud: 'Seek what is correct and complete it.' The Hanafi school reconciles these two hadiths by applying the first to cases where the person has no predominant opinion, and the second to cases where he does.

Key Terms
شک (shakk): Doubt about the number of rak'ahs prayed, with equal possibility of two numbers.
ظن غالب (zann ghalib): Predominant conjecture or strong opinion that one is more likely correct.
استئناف (isti'naf): To begin the prayer anew from the beginning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what should a person do if they frequently doubt whether they prayed three or four rak'ahs and they have no predominant opinion (ghalib al-zann)?
A) Repeat the prayer from the beginning.
B) Build on certainty (yaqin) by assuming three rak'ahs and add one.
C) Build on the number they feel is more likely.
D) Perform sajda sahw and make salam.

Key Takeaway
The key distinction: first-time doubt requires repeating the prayer; frequent doubt is resolved by predominant conjecture or, failing that, by building on certainty with sajda sahw.

intermediate
8 min
Resolving Doubt in Prayer: First-Time vs Frequent Shak
Explaining the resolution of shak with distinction between first-time and frequent doubt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi method for resolving doubt in the number of rak'ahs and apply it to first-time and frequent doubt scenarios.

Core Idea
First-time doubt: restart; frequent doubt: follow dominant conjecture or certainty, then perform sujud al-sahw.

Doubt (shak) in the number of rak'ahs can unsettle a worshipper. The Hanafi school provides a clear method: distinguish between an isolated first-time doubt and a recurring one. This lesson unpacks the ruling based on the hadith of Abu Sa'id al-Khudri and its reconciliation with the hadith of Abdullah ibn Mas'ud.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of doubt
Determine whether the doubt has occurred for the first time or is a frequent occurrence. This distinction changes the ruling entirely.
2
Apply the rule for first-time doubt
If it is the very first time such doubt occurs, the prayer must be restarted from the beginning (ista'nafa). There is no building on conjecture or certainty.
This is derived from the effect (athar) cited in the text.
3
Apply the rule for frequent doubt
If doubt occurs often, the worshipper builds on his preponderant opinion (ghalib al-zann) if one exists. If not, he builds on certainty (yaqin) – i.e., the lesser number.
Preponderant opinion is formed by reflecting on the prayer.
4
Complete the prayer and perform sujud al-sahw
After building on conjecture or certainty, complete the prayer as normal, then perform two prostrations of forgetfulness (sujud al-sahw) before the salam.
The hadith of Abu Sa'id explicitly commands sujud al-sahw before salam.

He does not know whether he prayed three or four, and that is the first time it occurred to him, then he restarts the prayer. But if it occurs to him frequently, he builds on his preponderant opinion if he has one, and if he does not have an opinion, he builds on certainty. Chapter: Whoever says when he doubts and does not know how many he prayed, he repeats. (First volume, page 385, number 4422) We interpret this effect (athar) that if the doubt occurred for the first time, he prays from the beginning, and if doubt occurs repeatedly, he acts on preponderant opinion and acts on certainty. Lexicon: 'ista'nafa' means to pray from the beginning. [308] (12) And if doubt comes to him repeatedly, he will build on preponderant opinion. For example, he doubts between three and four rak'ahs, and his dominant assumption is that he prayed four, then he acts on the dominant assumption and says the salam. If there is no dominant assumption on either side, then three is certain, so he takes three as the base and adds one rak'ah to make it four, and also performs the prostration of forgetfulness. There is proof of this in the hadith: From Abu Sa'id al-Khudri who said: The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: 'When one of you doubts in his prayer and does not know whether he prayed three or four, let him cast aside the doubt and build upon what he is certain of, then perform two prostrations before the salam.' (Muslim, Bab al-Sahw fi al-Salat, p.211 no. 572; Abu Dawud, Bab Idha Ashaka fi al-Thintayn wa al-Thalath, p.154 no. 1024; similar in Tirmidhi, Bab fi man yashukku fi al-ziyada wa al-nuqsan, p.91 no. 398). In this hadith, it is stated that if there is no dominant assumption but doubt on both sides, then one builds on certainty. The other hadith indicates that one can act on dominant assumption: 'Abdullah (ibn Mas'ud) said: The Prophet (peace be upon him) prayed and said: 'When one of you doubts in his prayer, let him strive for the correct estimation and complete it, then perform two prostrations.' Another hadith: 'Let him look for what is most likely correct.' (Muslim, Bab man shakka fi salatihi fa lam yadri kam salla fa litrah al-shakk... p.211 no.572). From this hadith it is understood that one should reflect on how many rak'ahs he prayed so that he can act on the side of preponderant opinion. Footnote (a): He said: If one of you doubts in his prayer and does not know whether he prayed three or four, then let him leave the doubt and build on certainty, then perform two prostrations before the salam. (b) 'Abdullah ibn Mas'ud said: The Prophet prayed and said: If one of you doubts in his prayer, he should strive for the correct one and complete the prayer upon it, then perform two prostrations. Another hadith: One should look at which is closer to correctness.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage begins by addressing the ruling on doubt (shak) in the number of rak'ahs during prayer. It distinguishes between two scenarios: (1) the first time such doubt occurs, the worshipper must restart the prayer (ista'nafa); (2) if doubt occurs frequently, the worshipper builds on his preponderant opinion (ghalib al-zann) if he has one, otherwise he builds on certainty (yaqin). The text then provides a hadith from Abu Sa'id al-Khudri ordering the one who doubts between three and four to cast aside doubt and build on certainty, then perform sujud al-sahw. A second hadith from Abdullah ibn Mas'ud allows acting on preponderant opinion. The author reconciles these: the first applies when there is no dominant conjecture; the second when there is. The lesson clarifies that the Hanafi position is to apply the rule based on frequency: occasional doubt requires restart, frequent doubt uses conjecture or certainty.

Key Terms
شك (shakk): Uncertainty between two possibilities (e.g., three or four rak'ahs) with neither side predominant.
ظن غالب (ghalib al-zann): A preponderant opinion where one side is more likely based on reflection.
سجود السهو (sujud al-sahw): Two prostrations performed to compensate for errors or forgetfulness in prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person frequently doubts the number of rak'ahs and has no preponderant opinion, what should he do?
A) Restart the prayer from the beginning.
B) Build on the lesser number (certainty) and perform sujud al-sahw.
C) Build on the greater number and perform sujud al-sahw.
D) Continue with whatever comes to mind.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling on shak in prayer hinges on frequency: first-time doubt requires restart, frequent doubt calls for building on dominant conjecture or certainty, followed by sujud al-sahw.

Salah — Prayer

Rules for the Sick Person's Prayer (Salat al-Marid)
Explaining how a sick person prays when unable to stand, sit, or prostrate

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the hierarchy of prayer positions for a sick person and apply the ruling that prayer is not waived as long as consciousness and ability to gesture remain.

Core Idea
Prayer adapts to ability; when standing fails, sit; when sitting fails, gesture; but prayer is never dropped while conscious.

Illness does not exempt a Muslim from the obligation of prayer—it only modifies how the prayer is performed. This passage from the Hanafi tradition lays out a clear hierarchy: stand if you can, sit if you cannot, and if even sitting is impossible, pray lying down with gestures. The key principle is that ability determines form, but the prayer itself remains binding as long as the mind is present. Let us walk through the rulings and the proofs that support them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
State the primary obligation: prayer is fard 'ayn. Then identify the gradation: standing → sitting → lying on side with gestures. Understand that each step is triggered only when the previous is impossible.
The hadith of 'Imran ibn Husayn explicitly gives this order.
2
Analyze the proof from Quran
Two Quranic verses are cited: 'There is no blame on the blind, the lame, or the sick' (48:17) and 'Allah does not burden a soul beyond its capacity' (2:286). Explain that these establish the principle of ability-based obligation.
These verses are general proofs, not specific to prayer.
3
Examine the hadith proof
The hadith of 'Imran ibn Husayn provides the specific hierarchy. Note that it is a hadith ahad (zanni al-thubut) but sufficient for establishing a wajib ruling in Hanafi usul.
The imperative 'salli' indicates obligation; the conditionals create the ladder.
4
Understand the gesture (ima') rules
When even sitting is impossible, the sick person prays lying on the back or side and gestures with the head. The gesture for prostration must be lower than that for bowing. This is supported by an athar from Qatadah.
The distinction in gesture depth is a wajib element.
5
Apply the conditions and exceptions
Prayer is not dropped as long as consciousness remains. If the person cannot face the qibla, he prays in whatever direction possible. No object should be raised to the forehead for prostration—gesture suffices.
The text also mentions not raising something to prostrate on, which implies using a gesture instead.

۳۰۹ (۱) اذا تعذر على المريض القيام صلى قاعدا يركع ويسجد فان لم يستطع الركوع والسجود اومئ ايماء وجعل السجود اخفض من الركوع [۳۱۰] (٢) ولا يرفع
باب صلوة المريض )
ضروری نوٹ مریض کو اللہ نے گنجائش دی ہے کہ جتنی طاقت ہو اتنا کام کرے۔ اس سے زیادہ کا مکلف نہیں ہے۔ چنانچہ کھڑے ہوکر نماز نہ پڑھ سکتا ہو تو بیٹھ کر نماز پڑھے اور بیٹھ کر نہ پڑھ سکتا ہو تو لیٹ کر اشارہ سے پڑھے۔ البتہ جب تک ہوش و حواس ہے اور اشارہ کر کے نماز پڑھ سکتا ہے تو نماز ساقط نہیں ہوگی۔ دلیل یہ آیت ہے لیس على الاعمى حرج ولا على الاعرج حرج ولا على المريض حرج (الف) ( آیت ۷ اسورۃ الفتح (۴۸) اس آیت سے ثابت ہوا کہ قدرت کے مطابق آدمی کام کرتا رہے لا يكلف الله نفسا إلا وسعها ( آیت ۲۸۶ سورۃ البقرۃ ۲) اس آیت سے ثابت ہوا کہ وسعت سے زیادہ اللہ تعالی مکلف نہیں بناتے ۔ [۳۰۹) (۱) بیمار پر کھڑا ہونا متعذر ہو تو بیٹھ کر نماز پڑھے گا، رکوع اور سجدہ کرے گا، پس اگر رکوع اور سجدہ نہ کر سکتا ہو تو اشارہ کرے گا اور سجدہ زیادہ جھکائے گا رکوع سے۔
جو آدمی کھڑا نہ ہو سکتا ہو تو بیٹھ کر نماز پڑھے گا۔ اور بیٹھ کر رکوع اور سجدہ کرے گا۔ اور رکوع اور سجدہ بھی نہ کر سکتا ہو تو رکوع اور سجدہ کا اشارہ کرے گا۔ اور سجدہ کے لئے سر کو زیادہ جھکائے گا وجہ حدیث میں ہے عن عمران بن حصين قال كانت بی بواسیر فسألت رسول الله له عن الصلوة فقال صل قائما فان لم تستطع فقاعدا فان لم تستطع فعلی جنب (ب) ( بخاری شریف، باب اذالم يطق قاعد اصلی علی جب ص ۱۵۰ نمبر ۱۷ ارترمذی شریف ، باب ما جاء ان صلوۃ القاعد على النصف من صلوۃ القائم ص ۸۵ نمبر۳۷۲ را بو داؤد شریف ، باب ہی صلوۃ القاعدص ۱۴۴ نمبر ۹۵۲) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ اگر بیٹھ نہ سکتا ہو تو پہلو کے بل لیٹ کر نماز پڑھے۔ رکوع اور سجدہ کے لئے اشارہ کرے۔ اور سجدہ کے لئے رکوع سے زیادہ سر جھکائے اس کی دلیل یہ ہے قال على كل حال مستلقيا ومنحرفا فاذا استقبل القبلة وكان لا يستطيع الا ذلك فيومئ ايماء ويجعل سجوده اخفض من ركوعه (ج) (مصنف عبدالرزاق ، باب صلوۃ المریض ج ثانی ص ۴۷۵ نمبر ۴۱۳۵) اس اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ سجدہ کے لئے سر زیادہ جھکائے۔
اومی : اشارہ کرے۔
[۳۱۰] (۲) اور اپنے چہرے کی طرف کوئی چیز نہ اٹھائے جس پر سجدہ کرے۔
حاشیہ : (الف) اندھے پر کوئی حرج نہیں لنگڑے پر کوئی حرج نہیں اور مریض پر کوئی حرج نہیں ہے ( ب ) عمران بن حصین فرماتے ہیں کہ مجھے بواسیر کا مرض تھا میں نے حضور سے نماز کے بارے میں پوچھا تو آپ نے فرمایا کھڑے ہو کر نماز پڑھو۔ پس اگر طاقت نہ رکھتے ہو تو بیٹھ کر کے، پس اگر طاقت نہ رکھتے ہو تو پہلو کے بل نماز پڑھو ( ج ) حضرت قتادہ سے روایت ہے ہر حال میں کہ چت لیٹ کر کے یا قبلہ سے علاوہ کی حالت میں ہو ، پس جب کہ قبلے کا استقبال کرو اور نہ طاقت رکھتا ہو مگر اسی کی تو اشارہ کرے اشارہ کرنا۔ اور سجدہ کو زیادہ جھکائے رکوع سے۔
Chapter: Prayer of the Sick
309 (1) When standing is difficult for the sick person, he prays sitting, bowing and prostrating. If he cannot bow and prostrate, he gestures with his head, making his prostration lower than his bowing. [310] (2) And he does not raise...
Chapter: Prayer of the Sick )
Essential note: Allah has given concession to the sick to do as much as he has strength. He is not obligated beyond that. So if he cannot pray standing, he prays sitting; if he cannot pray sitting, he prays lying down with gestures. However, as long as he is conscious and can gesture, prayer is not dropped. The proof is the verse: 'There is no blame on the blind, nor on the lame, nor on the sick' (Surah al-Fath, 48:17). This verse establishes that a person acts according to ability. Another verse: 'Allah does not burden a soul beyond its capacity' (Surah al-Baqarah, 2:286) proves that Allah does not obligate beyond capacity. [309) (1) When standing is difficult for the sick person, he prays sitting, performing bowing and prostration, and if he cannot bow and prostrate, he gestures and makes his prostration lower than his bowing.
The person who cannot stand will pray sitting and will bow and prostrate while sitting. If he cannot even bow and prostrate, he will gesture for bowing and prostration, and for prostration he will lower his head more. The proof is the hadith: 'Imran ibn Husayn said: I had hemorrhoids and asked the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) about prayer. He said: Pray standing; if you cannot, then sitting; if you cannot, then on your side.' (Bukhari, Bab Idha lam yutiq qa'idan salla 'ala jamb, p.150 no. 17? [likely 1117]; Tirmidhi, Bab Ma ja'a anna salat al-qa'id 'ala al-nisf min salat al-qa'im, p.85 no.372; Abu Dawud, Bab fi salat al-qa'id, p.144 no.952). From this hadith it is understood that if he cannot sit, he prays lying on his side. He gestures for bowing and prostration, and for prostration he lowers his head more than for bowing. Its proof is: 'He said: In every state—lying on his back or turned away—when he faces the qibla and cannot do more than that, he gestures and makes his prostration lower than his bowing.' (Musannaf 'Abd al-Razzaq, Bab salat al-marid, vol. 2 p.475 no.4135). From this effect it is understood that he lowers his head more for prostration.
'Ima' means to gesture.
[310] (2) And he should not raise anything towards his face on which to prostrate.
Footnotes: (a) 'There is no blame on the blind, nor on the lame, nor on the sick.' (b) 'Imran ibn Husayn said: I had hemorrhoids and asked the Prophet (peace be upon him) about prayer. He said: Pray standing; if you cannot, then sitting; if you cannot, then pray on your side.' (c) Narrated from Qatadah: 'In every state, whether lying on his back or turned away, when he faces the qibla and cannot do more than that, he gestures and makes his prostration lower than his bowing.'
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage introduces the chapter on the prayer of the sick (salat al-marid). It begins by stating the ruling: if standing is difficult, the sick person prays sitting, with bowing and prostration; if unable to bow and prostrate, he gestures with his head, making the gesture for prostration lower than that for bowing. The author emphasizes that Allah does not obligate beyond one's capacity, citing verses 48:17 and 2:286. The hadith of 'Imran ibn Husayn provides the hierarchy: standing, then sitting, then lying on the side. The text further explains that gestures (ima') are used when even sitting is impossible, and the prostration gesture must be lower than the bowing gesture. This is supported by an athar from Qatadah. Finally, the text notes that one should not raise an object to prostrate on (i.e., to rest the forehead) if it can be avoided; the gesture suffices. The lesson clarifies that prayer is not waived as long as consciousness and ability to gesture remain.

Key Terms
إيماء (ima'): Gesture with the head to indicate bowing and prostration when physical movements are not possible.
قاعدا (qa'idan): Sitting position in prayer, used when standing is difficult.
مستلقيا (mustalqiyan): Lying on the back, a position for prayer when unable to sit or lie on side.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is the correct order of prayer positions for a sick person according to the Hanafi text?
A) Standing, then sitting, then lying on side with gestures
B) Sitting, then standing, then lying on back
C) Lying on side, then sitting, then standing
D) Gestures only, without any position

Key Takeaway
The obligation of prayer is never dropped due to illness; only the method of performance adapts to the sick person's ability.

Prohibition of Raising an Object for Prostration When Sick
Ruling that the sick should not raise an object to prostrate on

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling (makruh tahrimi) on raising an object for prostration during sickness and explain the evidence from the hadith and the practice of Ibn Umar.

Core Idea
A sick person must not raise an object to prostrate; instead gesture with the head, making sujud lower than ruku.

When illness prevents prostrating on the ground, some might think using a pillow or block is acceptable. However, based on the practice of the Companion Ibn Umar and a prophetic hadith, the Hanafi school holds that raising anything to the forehead is prohibitively disliked. The proper method is to indicate sujud by a head gesture, with a lower inclination than for ruku. This lesson unpacks the ruling, its proof, and its application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Pinpoint the specific action being ruled upon: raising an object to the face for prostration when sick. Determine the hukm level from the text: makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked).
Makruh tahrimi means the act is sinful, but the prayer remains valid.
2
Locate the dalil (evidence)
The author cites two proofs: (1) the practice of Ibn Umar (athar) directing not to raise anything and to gesture, and (2) the hadith 'Make your sujud lower than your ruku' from Sunan al-Bayhaqi.
Both are classified as ahad (solitary) narration, providing zanni certainty.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah (indicative link)
The hadith commands that the sujud gesture be lower than the ruku gesture. This implies that the sujud is performed solely by head movement; raising an object is unnecessary and therefore prohibited (makruh tahrimi).
The athar of Ibn Umar explicitly forbids raising anything.
4
Note the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
The Hanafi position is clear, and there is no significant intra-madhhab dispute. The ruling applies to any object raised for prostration, and if done, the prayer is valid but sinful.
Other schools may permit it under necessity, but the Hanafi view is based on direct evidence.

[310] (2) And he should not raise anything to his face to prostrate on. Footnote: (a) Ibn Umar used to say: If any of you is sick and cannot prostrate on the ground, then do not raise anything to your face, and make your sujud like ruku, and gesture with your head. (b) In Sunan al-Bayhaqi, chapter on gesturing for ruku and sujud... In this hadith is: Make your sujud lower than your ruku. From this hadith and athar, it is understood that he should not raise anything to his face, but rather pray with head gestures, bowing less for ruku and more for sujud.
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage gives a supplementary ruling for the sick person who cannot prostrate on the ground: he should not bring an object (like a piece of wood or a pillow) to his forehead to prostrate on. Instead, he should simply gesture with his head, making the gesture for sujud lower than for ruku. The proof is the practice of Ibn Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) and a hadith. In the Hanafi school, this is a prohibited (makruh tahrimi) action because it deviates from the proper method of gesture.

Key Terms
رفع (raf'): Raising an object to the face for prostration.

Prayer of the Sick Lying on the Back
Ruling for the sick person who cannot sit on his back

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a sick person who cannot sit and explain the proof from the hadith of Ibn Umar.

Core Idea
When a sick person cannot sit, prayer on the back with feet toward qiblah is valid and preferred.

This lesson covers the final stage of prayer for the sick: when even sitting is impossible. The author prescribes lying on the back with feet toward the qiblah, gesturing for ruku and sujud. Although pointing feet toward qiblah is generally disliked, facing the qiblah is an obligation that takes priority. The ruling is supported by an athar from Ibn Umar, making it the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and ruling
Determine the condition: the sick person cannot sit. The ruling: lie on the back, place feet toward qiblah, and gesture for ruku and sujud.
This is the third stage of prayer for the sick, after standing and sitting.
2
Analyze the benefit and karahah
Facing the qiblah is obligatory for the healthy, so this method achieves that. Despite the general dislike of pointing feet toward qiblah, the obligation overrides the karahah, making this method superior.
The author (al-Sameri) explicitly prefers this method for this reason.
3
Examine the proof
The athar from Ibn Umar (Sunan al-Bayhaqi) directly instructs: 'The sick person prays lying on his back, his feet toward the qiblah.' This is a hadith ahad with zanni transmission and meaning, but it suffices as proof for this ruling.
No other proof is cited; the Hanafi school relies on this narration.
4
Note the Hanafi position
This is the mufta bihi (relied-upon) position. There is no intra-Hanafi dispute mentioned; it is consistent with zahir al-riwayah.
The authority weight is zahir al-riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۳۱۱] (۳) اگر بیٹھنے کی طاقت نہ رکھتا ہو تو گدی کے بل چت لیٹے اور دونوں پاؤں کو قبلہ کی طرف کرے اور رکوع اور سجدہ کا اشارہ کرے۔ ان چت لیٹ کر پاؤں کو قبلہ کی طرف کرے گا تو اس میں ایک فائدہ یہ ہے کہ قبلہ رخ ہوگا۔ جو نمازی کے لئے صحت کی حالت میں فرض ہے۔ اگر چہ ایک کراہیت بھی ہے کہ پاؤں قبلہ کی طرف ہوئے۔ حضرت مصنف نے قبلہ رخ کی وجہ سے اس طریق کو افضل قرار دیا ہے۔ اثر میں ہے عن ابن عمر قال يصلى المريض مستلقيا على قفاه تلى قدماه القبلة (ب) سنن سبیھقی ، باب روی فی کیفیۃ الصلوة على الجنب اوالاستلقاء وفیہ نظرج ثانی، ص۴۳۶، نمبر ۳۶۷۹)
حاشیہ : (ب) آپ نے فرمایا بیمار گدی کے بل چت لیٹ کر نماز پڑھے گا۔ اس کے دونوں قدم قبلہ کی طرف ہوں گے۔
[311] (3) If he cannot even sit, he should lie on his back (on the occiput) and place both feet toward the qiblah, and gesture for ruku and sujud. Lying on the back with feet toward the qiblah has the benefit of facing the qiblah, which is obligatory for the one who is healthy. Although there is some dislike (karahah) in pointing the feet toward the qiblah, the author (al-Sameri?) considered this method superior due to facing the qiblah. The athar: from Ibn Umar: The sick person prays lying on his back with his feet toward the qiblah. (b) Sunan al-Bayhaqi... Footnote: (b) He said: The sick person will pray lying on his back, his two feet toward the qiblah.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage provides the ruling for a sick person who cannot even sit: he should lie on his back (on the occiput) with his feet pointing toward the qiblah, and gesture for ruku and sujud. The proof is an athar from Ibn Umar. The author notes that although pointing feet toward the qiblah is generally disliked, facing the qiblah takes precedence, making this method preferable. In the Hanafi school, this is the recommended method when unable to sit.

Key Terms
استلقاء (istilqa'): Lying on the back, on the occiput, facing upward.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Prayer on the Right Side: The Preferred Position for the Sick
Ruling that lying on the right side with face toward qiblah is also valid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the preferred position for a sick person's prayer when unable to sit, and cite the hadith proof from Ali (ra).

Core Idea
If a sick person cannot sit, prayer on the right side is preferred over lying on the back.

This passage clarifies the sequence of prayer postures for a sick person. When sitting is impossible, lying on the side – specifically the right side – is the next best option, and it is valid. The hadith from Ali (ra) provides the authoritative order, distinguishing between lying on the side and lying on the back as separate steps. In the Hanafi school, this hierarchy is relied upon for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is performing salah while lying on the side (preferably right). The ruling is that it is sahih (valid) and preferred over lying on the back.
The ruling applies when the sick person cannot sit at all.
2
Locate the dalil
The hadith from Ali (ra) is cited from Sunan al-Bayhaqi and Dar Qutni. It explicitly lists the order of postures for the sick.
The hadith is ahad (single-transmitter) but accepted in Hanafi fiqh.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The hadith states: if cannot sit, pray on right side; if cannot, then on back. This establishes that lying on the side is the next step after sitting, and the back is last resort.
The wording 'pray on his right side' implies preference for that side.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
No intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf is mentioned; the ruling is consistent across the madhhab. Other schools may not specify the side or order, but here the Hanafi position is clear.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
This hadith is considered reliable and forms the basis of the mufta bihi position: lying on the right side is preferred, and lying on the back is only when that is not possible.
The authority weight is zahir al-riwayah.

[۳۱۲] (۴) اور اگر پہلو کے بل لیٹا اور اس کا چہرہ قبلہ کی طرف ہو اور اشارہ کرے تب بھی جائز ہے۔ مسئلہ نمبر میں بخاری کی حدیث گزری فان لم يستطع فعلی جنب کہ بیٹھنے کی قدرت نہ ہو تو پہلو کے بل لیٹ کر نماز پڑھے (۲) علی بن ابی طالب عن النبي الله قال يصلى المريض قائما ان استطاع فإن لم يستطع صلى قاعدا فان لم يستطع ان يسجد أومـاً وجعل سجوده اخفض من ركوعه فان لم يستطع ان يصلى قاعدا صلى على جنبه الايمن مستقبل القبلة فان لم يستطع ان يصلى على جنبه الأيمن صلى مستلقيا رجله مما يلى القبلة (ج) (سن للبیھقی ، باب ما روی فی کیفیۃ الصلوۃ علی الجنب اول استلقاء ، ج ثانی، ص۳۰۶ نمبر ۳۶۷۸ ردار قطنی ، باب صلوة المريض ومن رعف في صلوتہ الخ ، ج ثانی، ص ۳۱ نمبر ۱۶۹۰) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ دائیں پہلو کے بل لیٹ کر نماز پڑھے۔ اگر اس پر نماز نہ پڑھ سکتا ہو تب چت لیٹ کر قبلہ کی طرف

[312] (4) And if he lies on his side and his face is toward the qiblah and he gestures, that is also permissible. In the previous issue, the hadith from Bukhari passed: 'if you cannot, then on the side' that if he cannot sit, he prays lying on his side. (2) From Ali ibn Abi Talib from the Prophet (peace be upon him): The sick person prays standing if able; if not, sitting; if he cannot prostrate, he gestures and makes his sujud lower than his ruku; if he cannot pray sitting, he prays on his right side facing the qiblah; if he cannot pray on his right side, he prays lying on his back with his foot toward the qiblah. (c) Sunan al-Bayhaqi ... From this hadith it is understood that he prays lying on his right side. If he cannot pray on that, then lying on his back toward the qiblah. Footnote: (c) He said: The sick person prays standing if able; if not, sitting; if he cannot prostrate, he gestures and makes sujud lower; if he cannot sit, he prays on his right side facing the qiblah; if he cannot, he prays lying on his back with feet toward the qiblah.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage presents the final ruling: if the sick person can lie on his side (preferably the right side) with face toward the qiblah, that is valid. The hadith from Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) outlines a sequential order: standing, sitting, gesturing while sitting, then lying on the right side, and finally lying on the back. This establishes that the method of lying on the side is preferred over lying on the back, as it more closely resembles the normal prayer posture. In the Hanafi school, this hierarchy is followed.

Key Terms
على جنبه (ala janbihi): Lying on the side, specifically the right side as per the hadith.
أومـاً (iima'): Gesturing with the head or body as a substitute for bowing and prostrating.
مستلقيا (mustalqiyan): Lying on one's back, with face upward and feet toward the qiblah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Lying on the back
C) Lying on the left side
D) Gesturing while standing

Key Takeaway
When sitting is impossible, lying on the right side facing the qiblah is the preferred position for prayer, as established by the hadith of Ali (ra).

Prayer of Sick Person: Lying on Back When Cannot Sit
Ruling on the posture for prayer when unable to sit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the preferred posture for a sick person unable to sit in Hanafi fiqh and explain the dalil (evidence) and rationale behind it.

Core Idea
When unable to sit, lying on back with feet to Qibla and gesturing is the preferred way to pray, ensuring facing Qibla.

This passage addresses a common concern: how does a sick person who cannot even sit perform salah? The Hanafi school provides a clear method based on the practice of Ibn Umar. The author explains why lying on the back is considered superior despite a minor disliked aspect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act? The prayer of a sick person who cannot sit. The ruling prescribes a specific method, not a general permission.
The act is salah, the ruling is sunnah muakkadah (recommended) for this method.
2
State the detailed method
The sick person must lie on his back (specifically on the back of the neck), place both feet towards the Qibla, and gesture with the head for ruku' and sujud.
Gesturing for sujud must be lower than for ruku'.
3
Understand the benefit and the disliked aspect
Lying on the back ensures the body faces the Qibla, which is obligatory for the healthy. Although pointing feet towards the Qibla is generally disliked (makruh), this benefit overrides the dislike.
The author (al-Sameri) declares this method afdal (superior) due to facing Qibla.
4
Locate the proof
The dalil is the athar of Ibn Umar: 'The sick person prays lying on his back with his feet towards the Qibla.' (Sunan al-Bayhaqi)
This is a hadith ahad with zanni thubut and dalalah, but sufficient for a recommended practice.
5
Note the cross-madhhab dispute
Imam al-Shafi'i prefers lying on the right side (like one facing the Qibla). The Hanafi tarjih prefers the back due to the explicit hadith and the benefit of facing Qibla.
The practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) is the actual posture adopted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۳۱۱) (۳) فان لم يستطع القعود استلقى على قفاه وجعل رجليه الى القبلة واومى بالركوع والسجود

اگر بیٹھنے کی طاقت نہ رکھتا ہو تو گدی کے بل چت لیٹے اور دونوں پاؤں کو قبلہ کی طرف کرے اور رکوع اور سجدہ کا اشارہ کرے۔ ان چت لیٹ کر پاؤں کو قبلہ کی طرف کرے گا تو اس میں ایک فائدہ یہ ہے کہ قبلہ رخ ہوگا۔ جو نمازی کے لئے صحت کی حالت میں فرض ہے۔ اگر چہ ایک کراہیت بھی ہے کہ پاؤں قبلہ کی طرف ہوئے۔ حضرت مصنف نے قبلہ رخ کی وجہ سے اس طریق کو افضل قرار دیا ہے۔ اثر میں ہے عن ابن عمر قال يصلى المريض مستلقيا على قفاه تلى قدماه القبلة (ب) سنن سبیھقی ، باب روی فی کیفیۃ الصلوة على الجنب اوالاستلقاء وفیہ نظرج ثانی، ص۴۳۶، نمبر ۳۶۷۹)
If he cannot sit, he should lie on his back, place both his feet towards the Qibla, and gesture with his head for bowing and prostration. Explanation: If he cannot sit, he should lie supine on the back of his neck and place both feet towards the Qibla and gesture for bowing and prostration. When he lies on his back with feet towards Qibla, one benefit is that he will be facing the Qibla, which is obligatory for the one in health. Although there is a dislike in pointing feet towards the Qibla, the author (al-Sameri) considered this method superior due to facing the Qibla. There is an athar: Ibn Umar said, 'The sick person prays lying on his back with his feet towards the Qibla.' (Sunan al-Bayhaqi, Bab…).
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling for the prayer of a sick person who cannot sit. The matn states that such a person should lie on his back, place his feet toward the Qibla, and gesture with his head for ruku' and sujud. The sharh explains that lying on the back with feet to the Qibla ensures facing the Qibla, which is obligatory for the healthy. Although pointing feet toward the Qibla is generally disliked, the author declares this method superior because it achieves facing the Qibla. The hadith of Ibn Umar supports this practice. The passage also implicitly refutes the notion that one should raise an object to the face for prostration, as mentioned in prior commentary.

Key Terms
استلقى على قفاه (istalqa 'ala qafah): To lie on one's back, specifically on the back of the neck, supine with feet extended.
ايماء (ima'): Gesturing with the head in place of actual bowing and prostration when unable to perform them physically.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school prefers lying on the back with feet to Qibla for the sick, prioritizing facing Qibla over the dislike of pointing feet.

Prayer of Sick Person: Lying on Side When Cannot Sit
Ruling on lying on the side as an alternative posture for prayer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on lying on the side for a sick person who cannot sit, explain the preference for lying on the back, and identify the cross-madhhab dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i.

Core Idea
Lying on the side is permissible, but lying on the back is preferred to maximize facing the Qibla.

In the previous lesson, we covered the sick person who can sit. Now we consider those who cannot sit but can lie down. The matn presents a straightforward ruling: lying on any side with face to Qibla and gesturing is valid. However, the commentary by al-Sameri reveals a nuance: the Hanafi school prefers lying on the back over the side, and this preference is based on a hadith from Ibn Umar. In this lesson, we will explore the ruling, its proof, and the cross-madhhab difference with Imam al-Shafi'i.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling
Identify the exact ruling: Lying on the side with face toward Qibla and making gestures is permissible (jā'iz).
The matn does not differentiate between right or left side.
2
Identify the preferred posture
The commentary notes that the author (al-Sameri) considers lying on the back as afdal (better) because it allows full alignment with the Qibla. This is the relied-upon Hanafi view.
Lying on the back is not the only valid posture, but it is the most recommended.
3
Locate the dalil
The Hanafi proof is the hadith of Ibn Umar: 'The sick person prays lying on his back with his feet towards the Qibla.' This is a zanni al-thubut hadith but sufficient for preference.
The Shafi'i school uses a hadith of Ali preferring the right side.
4
Understand the ikhtilaf
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that the right side is preferred over the back. The Hanafi response prioritizes facing Qibla directly, which the back posture achieves best.
The practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf): a Hanafi lies on his back, a Shafi'i on his right side.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۳۱۲) (۴) وان اضطجع على جنبه ووجهه قبل القبلة واومئ جاز [۳۱۳] (۵) فان لم يستطع الايماء برأسه اخر الصلوة ولا يومئ بعينيه ولا بقلبه ولا بحاجبيه ... فان امام شافعی کے نزدیک یہی ہے کہ دائیں پہلو کے بل لیٹ کر نماز پڑھے اور وہ نہ کر سکتا ہو تو لیٹ کر قبلہ رخ پاؤں کرے۔ ان کی دلیل یہی دونوں احادیث ہیں۔
And if he lies on his side with his face towards the Qibla and gestures, it is permissible. Then if he cannot gesture with his head, he should delay the prayer and not gesture with his eyes, nor his heart, nor his eyebrows… For Imam al-Shafi'i, the view is that he prays on his right side, and if he cannot, then he lies on his back with feet towards Qibla. Their evidence is these two hadiths.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage gives the ruling for a sick person who cannot sit but can lie on his side. The matn says it is permissible to lie on one's side with face towards Qibla and gesture. The sharh then explains that Imam al-Shafi'i holds the opposite preference: the right side is preferred over lying on the back. The Hanafi view, as stated earlier, prefers lying on the back because it better faces the Qibla. The passage also introduces the next ruling (5) about inability to gesture, which is part of the same legal discussion but will be treated separately.

Key Terms
اضطجع على جنبه (idtaja'a 'ala janbih): To lie on one's side, preferably the right side according to some scholars.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Lying on the right side
B) Lying on the back
C) Lying on the left side
D) Lying prone

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits a sick person who cannot sit to pray lying on any side, but considers lying on the back most recommended for optimal Qibla orientation.
