Sick Person Unable to Gesture with Head: Prayer Postponed
Ruling for when a sick person cannot gesture with head

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a sick person who cannot gesture with their head and apply the prohibition of gestures with other body parts.

Core Idea
If head gestures are impossible, prayer is postponed – no eye, heart, or eyebrow gestures allowed.

When a person is so ill that they cannot even move their head to indicate bowing and prostration, some may think they can substitute with gestures of the eyes or heart. This lesson clarifies the Hanafi position: only head gestures are accepted; if impossible, the prayer is delayed until recovery. The obligation remains due to the presence of intellect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the situation
Determine that the sick person is unable to gesture with the head (ima' bil-ra's) due to illness.
2
Recognize the prohibited alternatives
Note that gesturing with eyes, heart, or eyebrows is explicitly forbidden in the text.
3
Apply the ruling: postponement
Since no valid substitution exists, the prayer is postponed (akhkhara al-salah) and must be performed later when the person can gesture with the head.
The obligation remains wajib; only timing changes.
4
Understand the underlying proof
The proof is based on hadith that limit ima' to the head and the principle that the obligation continues as long as intellect remains.
Dalil is implied: the restriction of ima' to head is established from Sunnah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۳۱۳] (۵) فان لم يستطع الايماء برأسه اخر الصلوة ولا يومئ بعينيه ولا بقلبه ولا بحاجبيه

اگر سر سے بھی اشارہ کرنے کی طاقت نہ ہو تو نماز مؤخر ہوگی۔ چونکہ عقل دماغ موجود ہے اس لئے شریعت کا خطاب اس پر موجود ہے اس لئے نماز لازم ہوگی۔ البتہ مؤخر کر کے نماز پڑھے گا۔
Then if he cannot gesture with his head, he postpones the prayer, and he does not gesture with his eyes, nor with his heart, nor with his eyebrows. If he cannot even gesture with his head, then the prayer is postponed. Since the intellect is present, the address of Shariah is upon him, so the prayer is obligatory. However, he will pray after postponing.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This ruling addresses the extreme case where the sick person cannot even move the head to gesture. The matn clearly says: do not resort to gesturing with eyes, heart, or eyebrows. Instead, the prayer should be postponed (akhkhara al-salah) until the person recovers enough to perform the gestures. The sharh explains that despite the inability, the obligation remains because the intellect is intact; therefore the prayer is not dropped but delayed. The evidence for this is implied from hadith that limit ima' to head movements, and from the principle that the address of Shariah continues for those with reason.

Key Terms
ايماء بالرأس (ima' bil-ra's): Gesturing with the head as a substitute for bowing and prostration when physically unable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what should a sick person do if they cannot gesture with their head during prayer?
A) Gesture with their eyes
B) Gesture with their heart
C) Postpone the prayer until they can gesture with their head
D) Gesture with their eyebrows

Key Takeaway
The obligation of prayer persists even when physical motion is impossible; if head gestures are not possible, the prayer is postponed until they become possible.

Standing When Unable to Bow or Prostrate
Ruling and rationale for a sick person who can stand but cannot bow/prostrate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a sick person who can stand but cannot bow or prostrate, and explain the rational principle that the means drops when the end is impossible.

Core Idea
Standing is only required if it enables ruku' and sujud; if not, it is optional.

When a sick person can stand but cannot physically perform ruku' and sujud, a common question arises: must he still stand? This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling that standing is no longer obligatory, and the rational principle behind it. The ruling illustrates that obligations are contingent on ability, and the means falls away when the end cannot be achieved.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and impediment
The act is standing in prayer (qiyam). The impediment is the inability to perform ruku' and sujud despite being able to stand.
The person can stand but cannot bend due to pain or illness.
2
State the ruling
The matn states: 'لم يلزمه القيام وجازان يصلى قاعدا يومئ ايماء' – standing is not obligatory; it is permissible to pray sitting and gesture.
The ruling is clear: standing is not required.
3
Analyze the rationale
The sharh explains that standing was prescribed to enable proper ruku' and sujud. When that purpose cannot be achieved, the obligation of standing is lifted.
This is a rational (dalil aqli) proof: means are only required when they serve their end.
4
Conclude the legal effect
The person may choose to stand anyway, but it is not obligatory. He may also sit and gesture for ruku' and sujud.
The ruling provides flexibility while maintaining the integrity of the prayer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۱۳۱۴] (۶) فان قدر على القيام ولم يقدر على الركوع والسجود لم يلزمه القيام وجازان يصلى قاعدا يومئ ايماء

اگر کھڑے ہونے پر قدرت رکھتا ہو لیکن رکوع اور سجدے پر قدرت نہ رکھتا ہو تو اسکو کھڑا ہونا لازم نہیں ہے۔ اور اس کے لئے جائز ہے کہ بیٹھ کر اشارہ سے نماز پڑھے۔ تشریح ایک آدمی کھڑا تو ہو سکتا ہے لیکن پیٹھ میں درد کی وجہ سے رکوع سجدہ نہیں کر سکتا تو اس کے لئے کھڑا ہونا ضروری نہیں ہے۔ وہ بیٹھ کر رکوع اور سجدے کا اشارہ کر کے نماز پڑھے۔ اس کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ کھڑا ہونا اس لئے تھا تا کہ صحیح طور پر رکوع اور سجدہ کر سکے۔ لیکن جب رکوع اور سجدہ ہی نہیں کر سکا تو کھڑا ہونا جو فرض تھا اس سے ساقط ہو جائے گا۔ اب چاہے تو کھڑا ہو چاہے تو بیٹھ کر اشارہ سے نماز پڑھے۔
Then if he is capable of standing but not capable of bowing and prostration, standing is not obligatory upon him, and it is permissible for him to pray sitting and gesture. If a person can stand but cannot bow and prostrate due to back pain, then standing is not necessary. He may pray sitting, gesturing for bowing and prostration. The reason is that standing was prescribed in order to perform ruku' and sujud properly. But when he cannot perform ruku' and sujud, the obligation of standing falls away. He may either stand or sit and gesture.
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage resolves a common situation: a person can stand but cannot bend for ruku' or sujud. The matn states that standing is not obligatory (la yalzimuhu al-qiyam); it is permissible to sit and gesture. The sharh provides the rationale: standing is a means to enable proper ruku' and sujud. Since the end cannot be achieved, the means (standing) is no longer required. This is a clear application of the principle that obligations are contingent on ability. The person may choose to stand anyway, but it is not required.

Key Terms
قدر على القيام (qadara 'ala al-qiyam): Physically able to stand upright without harm.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A sick person who can stand but cannot bow or prostrate should:
A) Stand and perform the prayer in standing position
B) Sit and gesture for ruku' and sujud
C) Lie on his back and gesture
D) Postpone the prayer

Key Takeaway
Standing becomes optional when a person cannot perform ruku' and sujud, because the obligation of standing is a means to an end.

Prayer of a Healthy Person Who Falls Ill Mid-Prayer
Ruling on completing a prayer when health deteriorates during the prayer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling when a healthy person falls ill during prayer, determining the correct posture for completion.

Core Idea
Prayer posture adapts to ability mid-prayer – the obligation adjusts to new circumstances.

This passage addresses a common scenario: a person begins the prayer in full health, standing as required, but then becomes ill during the prayer. Does he continue standing in pain, or may he adapt? The Hanafi school provides a clear ruling that respects both the obligation of prayer and the mercy of ease. Understanding this ruling helps the believer maintain the sanctity of prayer without unnecessary hardship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is completing a prayer after health changes mid-prayer. The ruling: complete sitting, bowing and prostrating if possible, otherwise gesturing, or lying on back if cannot sit.
The ruling applies retroactively from the point of illness, not from the start.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The primary dalil is the hadith: 'Pray standing; if you cannot, then sitting; if you cannot, then on your side' (Sahih al-Bukhari). Also the Quranic principle: 'Allah does not burden a soul beyond its capacity' (2:286).
The hadith is ahad but the meaning is supported by ijma and the general concession for the sick.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The hadith orders prayer in the best possible posture, but permits lowering the posture if unable. The command 'pray standing' is conditional on ability. When ability changes, the commanded posture changes accordingly. The jurists extended this to mid-prayer by qiyas: the same principle of ability applies throughout the prayer.
The Hanafi school does not require breaking the prayer and restarting; continuation is valid.
4
Note ikhtilaf (if any)
Some early scholars held that if a person becomes unable to stand, they should break and restart in the new posture. The Hanafi school, however, allows continuation based on the general principle that the prayer is one continuous act and the obligation adapts.
The Hanafi position is considered stronger because it avoids unnecessary repetition.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi position is mufta bihi and is relied upon by the majority of later Hanafi scholars. It is based on ease (taysir) and the hadith's flexibility.
No explicit tarjih authority mentioned in this text, but it is part of zahir al-riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۱۳۱۵] (۷) فان صلی الصحيح بعض صلوته قائما ثم حدث به مرض اتمها قاعدا يركع ويسجد ويومئ ايماء ان لم يقدر على الركوع والسجود۔ او استلقى ان لم يقدر على القعود۔

اگر تندرست آدمی نے بعض نماز کھڑے ہو کر پڑھی پھر اس کو مرض پیدا ہوا تو اس کو پوری کرے گا بیٹھ کر کے، رکوع کریگا اور سجدہ کریگا، اور اشارہ کرے گا اگر رکوع اور سجدے پر طاقت نہ رکھتا ہو۔ یا چت لیٹے گا اگر بیٹھنے کی طاقت نہ رکھتا ہو۔
Then if a healthy person prayed part of his prayer standing, then illness befell him, he completes it sitting, bowing and prostrating, and gesturing if he is unable to bow and prostrate, or he lies on his back if he is unable to sit. If a healthy person has performed part of his prayer standing, then he falls ill, he will complete it sitting, performing ruku' and sujud, and gesturing if he cannot do ruku' and sujud, or he will lie on his back if he cannot sit.
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage deals with a change in health during the prayer. A person starts the prayer standing (as required for the healthy) but then becomes ill, e.g., suddenly weak or in pain. The matn rules that he should complete the rest of the prayer in the posture appropriate to his new condition. If he can sit, he continues sitting with actual ruku' and sujud if possible; if not, he gestures. If he cannot sit, he lies on his back. This is based on the principle that the prayer is performed according to ability, and the obligation adapts to the changing circumstances. The previous hadith from Bukhari (mentioned in the commentary) and the general concession for the sick support this.

Key Terms
اتمها قاعدا (atammaha qa'idan): To complete the remaining part of the prayer in a sitting position.
ايماء (ima'): Gesturing with the head for ruku' and sujud when unable to perform them physically.
استلقى (istalqa): To lie on one's back, the final position if unable to sit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person starts a prayer standing and then becomes ill and cannot stand, he should:
A) Break the prayer and restart later
B) Continue standing even if in pain
C) Complete the prayer sitting and bow/prostrate if possible
D) Lie on his back immediately

Key Takeaway
A healthy person who falls ill during prayer must complete it in the posture their new ability allows, from sitting to gestures to lying down.

Postponing Prayer When Unable to Nod
Ruling on postponement for unable to nod

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a severely ill person who cannot indicate with the head, and explain why the prayer is postponed rather than performed with minimal gestures.

Core Idea
If the head cannot move, prayer is postponed—no gesturing with eyes, heart, or eyebrows.

This passage addresses the extreme case of a sick person who cannot even nod for bowing and prostration. The Hanafi school requires physical movement; when that is impossible, the prayer must be delayed until ability returns. The text explicitly forbids substituting with the heart, eyes, or eyebrows, and contrasts with the Shafi'i view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The ruling applies to a person who cannot indicate with the head during prayer. The text says prayer is postponed (akhkhar al-salah). This is a legal proscription: the prayer becomes wajib to delay, not to perform with insufficient gestures.
The act is the postponement itself, not the prayer; the prayer obligation remains but its execution is deferred.
2
Locate the dalil
The Hanafi proof is the general hadith: 'Pray standing; if unable, sitting; if unable, on your side; if unable, then however you can' (implied). This hadith is understood to require actual movement; head nodding is the minimum.Merely thinking or moving eyebrows does not count as 'however you can'.
The Shafi'i rely on a different hadith about lying on the back with feet to Qibla.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The imperative 'pray' (salli) in the hadith implies action. The concession 'however you can' (fa-in lam yastati‘, etc.) still requires a perceivable movement. The head is the smallest manageable part for indication; lesser movements are not recognized as 'prayer'.
Hanafi usul: al-amr yufid al-wujub, and the concession restricts but does not eliminate the requirement of movement.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that if lying on the side is impossible, the sick person prays lying on the back with legs toward Qibla, gesturing with eyes, etc. The Hanafi respond that postponement maintains the honor of the prayer until full ability returns.
The dispute is over whether the prayer is 'done' or 'delayed'.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi position is mufta bihi in the school. The reasoning: the address of the Shari‘ah remains binding as long as the intellect is present, but fulfillment requires the minimal physical form. Postponement is better than a defective performance.
Tarjih authority: later commentators like Al-Marghinani affirm this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فان لم يستطع الايماء برأسه اخر الصلوة ولا يومئ بعينيه ولا بقلبه ولا بحاجبيه 
فان امام شافعی کے نزدیک یہی ہے کہ دائیں پہلو کے بل لیٹ کر نماز پڑھے اور وہ نہ کر سکتا ہو تو لیٹ کر قبلہ رخ پاؤں کرے۔ ان کی دلیل یہی دونوں احادیث ہیں۔ لغت استلقی : چت لیٹا، ففا [؟] : گدی۔ ۳۱۳۱ (۵) پس اگر سر سے اشارہ کرنے کی طاقت نہ ہو تو نماز مؤخر ہو جائے گی ، اور نہ اشارہ کرے اپنی آنکھوں سے اور نہ دل سے اور نہ بھؤوں سے ان اگر سر سے بھی اشارہ کرنے کی طاقت نہ ہو تو نماز مؤخر ہوگی ۔ چونکہ عقل دماغ موجود ہے اس لئے شریعت کا خطاب اس پر موجود ہے اس لئے نماز لازم ہوگی۔ البتہ مؤخر کر کے نماز پڑھے گا۔
مسئلہ نمبرہ کی حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ سر سے اشارہ کرے گا۔ اور سر سے اشارہ نہ کر سکے تو نماز مؤخر ہو جائے گی۔
بحاجبیہ : دونوں بھووں سے۔
If he cannot indicate with his head, he postpones the prayer, and he does not indicate with his eyes, nor with his heart, nor with his eyebrows.
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This is a ruling for the severely ill person who cannot even nod the head for bowing and prostration. The Hanafi position is that such a person postpones the prayer until he can indicate with his head or recovers enough. Indication with the eyes, heart, or eyebrows is not valid because these are not considered proper actions in prayer. The basis is that the prayer requires actual physical movement; mere mental intention or slight movement of other body parts does not fulfill the required actions. The Sharh mentions that Imam Shafi'i holds a different view: if unable to lie on the side, the sick person should stretch legs towards the Qibla. The Hanafi school relies on the hadith that the sick should pray as much as able, but if unable to indicate with the head, postponement is necessary because the address of Shari'ah remains valid as long as intellect is present. The term 'بحاجبيہ' means 'eyebrows'.

Key Terms
الايماء (al-īmā'): Indication with the head for bowing and prostration when unable to do the actual movements.
بحاجبيه (bi-ḥājibayhi): With his eyebrows, i.e., moving them to indicate.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in this passage, what should a severely ill person do if he cannot indicate with his head?
A) He should pray with his heart intentions only.
B) He should indicate with his eyebrows.
C) He should postpone the prayer until he can nod.
D) He should lie on his back and gesture with his eyes.

Key Takeaway
When physical movement for prayer is impossible, the prayer must be postponed; minimal gestures like blinking or thinking do not suffice.

Salah — Prayer

Building Upon Prayer When Illness Strikes Mid-Prayer
Ruling on completing prayer when a healthy person falls ill mid-prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on building upon a valid prayer when a healthy person becomes ill mid-prayer, distinguishing between valid continuation and invalidation due to posture change.

Core Idea
A valid prayer is not invalidated by a mid-prayer change of posture due to illness; build upon what remains.

In the Hanafi school, the prayer of a healthy person who becomes ill mid-prayer remains valid even when he changes posture. This ruling is based on the Quranic principle of not burdening the sick beyond their capacity and the hadith commanding prayer as able. In this lesson, we will unpack the proofs and the operation of building (bina) upon a higher level of standing with a lower level of sitting, and see how this differs from the view of some other schools.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Ruling
First, identify the specific act being discussed: a healthy person who prayed some rak'ahs standing then becomes ill. The ruling is that he does not restart but continues in the best posture he can manage (sitting, indicating, or lying).
The act is the continuation of the same prayer, not a new one.
2
Locate the Dalil (Proofs)
The passage cites two proofs: (1) The general Quranic principle that the sick are not burdened beyond their capacity. (2) The hadith from Bukhari that one should pray standing, if unable then sitting, if unable then lying on the side. The sharh states that the lower level is built upon the higher level.
The hadith is referenced as having passed earlier in the text (issue number one).
3
Trace the Wajh al-Dalalah (Legal Link)
The Quranic principle shows that obligation is scaled to ability: what was valid before the change remains valid, and the rest is performed according to current ability. The hadith directly permits the shift from standing to sitting mid-prayer if needed, because it allows the entire prayer to be performed sitting if standing is impossible at the start. By analogy (qiyas awla), if one can only do part of the prayer standing, it is even more permissible to build upon that part.
The key concept is building (bina) the lower upon the higher – this is permissible because the prayer's validity is not tied to a single posture.
4
Note the Ikhtilaf (Scholarly Disagreement)
Some other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) hold that a change in posture from standing to sitting without a valid excuse invalidates the prayer and requires restarting. However, in this case the change is due to a genuine excuse (illness), so even for them it may be excused. The Hanafi view is more permissive, allowing bina on the prior valid part.
The ikhtilaf is about whether a complete continuity of posture is required for the prayer's unity.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi Tarjih (Preponderance)
The Hanafi position is preferred because it follows the general principle of facilitation for the sick and the explicit textual permission to pray sitting. It also avoids unnecessary hardship of restarting a lengthy prayer.
This ruling is found in the Zahir al-Riwayah and is the mufta bihi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فان صلی الصحيح بعض صلوته قائما ثم حدث به مرض اتمها قاعدا يركع ويسجد ويومئ ايماء ان لم يستطع الركوع والسجود او مستلقيا ان لم يستطع القعود
پس اگر تندرست آدمی نے بعض نماز کھڑے ہو کر پڑھی پھر اس کو مرض پیدا ہوا تو اس کو پوری کرے گا بیٹھ کر کے، رکوع کریگا اور سجدہ کریگا، اور اشارہ کرے گا اگر رکوع اور سجدے پر طاقت نہ رکھتا ہو۔ یا چت لیٹے گا اگر بیٹھنے کی طاقت نہ رکھتا ہو۔
مسئلہ نمبرا میں بخاری کی حدیث گزر چکی ہے کہ کھڑے ہونے کی طاقت نہ رکھتا ہوتو پہلو کے بل لیٹ کر نماز پڑھے۔ اور یہ بھی گزرا کہ رکوع اور سجدہ نہ کر سکتا ہو تو اشارہ سے نماز پڑھے گا (۲) آیت میں گزرا کہ مریض پر کوئی حرج نہیں ہے۔ جتنے پر قدرت ہوگی اتنا ہی کرے گا۔ اس لئے کھڑے ہو کر نماز پڑھ رہا تھا اور درمیان میں زیادہ بیمار ہو گیا اور بیٹھ گیا تو ادنی کو اعلی پر بنا کیا اس لئے جائز ہے۔ اور رکوع سجدہ نہ کر سکا تو اشارہ سے نماز پڑھے گا۔
If a healthy person prayed part of his prayer standing, then an illness occurred to him, he completes it sitting, bowing and prostrating, and indicating if he cannot bow and prostrate, or lying on his back if he cannot sit.
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This ruling deals with a change of condition during prayer. A healthy person begins prayer standing, then mid-prayer becomes ill and cannot continue standing. He should continue the remainder of the prayer sitting, performing actual ruku' and sajdah if possible; if not, he makes head indications. If he cannot even sit, he lies on his back. This is based on the general principle that the sick are not burdened beyond their capacity (Quran: 'No harm upon the sick'), and the hadith that one should pray as much as able. The Sharh notes that the lower level (sitting) is built upon the higher level (standing), meaning the prayer is valid even though some parts were performed standing and some sitting. The prayer does not need to be restarted.

Key Terms
بنی (banā): To build upon; continue the prayer from where it left off rather than restarting.
قاعدا (qā'idan): Sitting; the posture of sitting during prayer.
يومئ إيماء (yūmī'u īmā'an): He indicates by head movements; a substitute for bowing and prostrating when unable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a healthy person starts praying standing, then becomes unable to stand mid-prayer due to illness, what should he do according to the Hanafi school?
A) Continue standing despite pain
B) Restart the prayer sitting
C) Complete the remaining part sitting, bowing/prostrating or indicating
D) Break the prayer and pray later

Key Takeaway
When a healthy person becomes ill mid-prayer, he builds upon his valid prayer by continuing in the best posture he can manage, without restarting.

Building Upon Prayer When Recovering from Sitting to Standing
Ruling with proof on building upon sitting prayer when health improves.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on building upon a prayer when recovering from sitting to standing and cite the supporting evidence.

Core Idea
Sitting is half-standing; recovery allows building, not restarting.

This lesson examines the case where a person begins prayer sitting due to illness, performing actual ruku' and sujood, then recovers mid-prayer and is able to stand. The Hanafi school permits continuing standing without restarting, based on the principle that sitting is considered 'half-standing' (adha qa'im) and supported by the Prophet's practice. We will explore the ruling, its rationale, and the evidence.

Guided Path
1
State the Ruling and Identify the Act
Identify the scenario: a person prays sitting due to illness, then recovers. The ruling is that he builds upon his prayer standing (بنى على صلوته قائما). This means he continues from where he left off, not restarting.
This is the reverse of the previous ruling where a standing person sits down.
2
Explain the Underlying Legal Principle
The Sharh explains that sitting is considered 'half-standing' (القاعد آدها قائم). Therefore, moving from sitting to standing is a permissible transition during prayer, analogous to moving from standing to sitting.
This principle is key to understanding why building is allowed.
3
Locate the Dalil (Evidence)
The hadith of Aisha (رضي الله عنها) is cited: the Prophet (ﷺ) would pray sitting, then when only a small portion of recitation remained, he would stand and complete it standing. This shows building from sitting to standing in nafl prayers.
The hadith is from Sahih Muslim, #731.
4
Show the Wajh al-Dalalah (Proof Indication)
The hadith explicitly demonstrates the act of building from sitting to standing. Although it narrates nafl prayer, the Hanafi school extends the ruling to fard by qiyas (analogy) because the effective cause (illah) is the change in ability, which applies equally.
Some schools may object that the hadith is about nafl, but the Hanafi response uses qiyas.
5
Note the Cross-Madhhab Status
This ruling is mukhtalaf fih. Some other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) require restarting the prayer if the posture changes from sitting to standing mid-prayer, as they consider standing an integral pillar that must be present from the start of each rak'ah.
Practical difference: Hanafis avoid hardship; others may demand a fresh start.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن صلى قاعدا يركع و يسجد لمرض ثم صح بنى على صلوته قائما
جس نے بیٹھ کر نماز پڑھی رکوع اور سجدہ کرتے ہوئے مرض کی بنا پر پھر تندرست ہو گیا تو کھڑے ہو کر اپنی نماز پر بنا کرے گا۔ (1) بیٹھنا آدھا کھڑا ہوتا ہے اس لئے اگر بیٹھا ہوار کوع و سجدہ کر رہا تھا اور کھڑے ہونے پر قدرت ہو گئی تو اسی پر بنا کرے گا اور باقی نماز کھڑے ہو کر پوری کرے گا (۲) کھڑے ہونے والے بیٹھنے والے کی اقتدا کر سکتے ہیں لیکن لیٹنے والے کی اقتدا نہیں کر سکتے اس سے بھی معلوم ہوا کہ بیٹھنا آدھا کھڑا ہونا ہے۔ اس لئے اس پر بنا کرے گا۔ شروع نماز سے پڑھنے کی ضرورت نہیں ہے (۳) حدیث میں اس کا ثبوت ہے عن عائشة ان رسول الله كان يصلى جالسا فيقرأ وهو جالس فاذا بقى من قرأته قدر ما يكون ثلثين او اربعين آية قام فقرأ وهو قائم ثم ركع ثم سجد ثم يفعل فى الثانية مثل ذلك (الف) ( مسلم شریف، باب جواز النافلة قائما و قاعدا ص ۲۵۲ نمبر ۷۳۱ ) اس حدیث میں آپ نے بیٹھ کر نماز پڑھی ہے پھر آخر میں کھڑے ہو کر اس پر بنا کیا ہے۔ یہ حدیث اگر چہ نوافل کے بارے میں ہے لیکن اس سے معلوم ہوتا ہے کہ بیماری کی صورت میں فرائض میں بھی بیٹھنے پر کھڑا ہونے کو بنا کر سکتا ہے۔
And whoever prayed sitting, bowing and prostrating due to illness, then he recovered, he builds upon his prayer standing.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This ruling is the reverse of the previous one: if a person prays sitting because of illness, performing actual ruku' and sajdah, then during the prayer recovers and is able to stand, he continues the remaining rak'ahs standing. He does not need to restart. The Sharh explains that sitting is considered 'half-standing' (adha qa'im), so one can build upon it. Evidence: the hadith of 'Aisha that the Prophet (peace be upon him) would pray sitting, and when a small portion of recitation remained, he would stand and complete it standing. Although this narration is about optional prayers, it indicates the principle is applicable to obligatory prayers by analogy (qiyas).

Key Terms
بنی (banā): To continue the prayer from where left off, rather than starting over.
قاعد (qā'id): Sitting posture during prayer.
قائم (qā'im): Standing posture during prayer.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, sitting is considered half-standing; recovery mid-prayer allows building upon the sitting portion without restart.

Restarting Prayer When Ability Returns After Head Indication
Ruling on restarting prayer when ability returns after head indication

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a person gains ability to bow and prostrate during a prayer performed with head indication, and explain the Hanafi reasoning for restarting.

Core Idea
Head indication is the weakest form; it cannot be built upon when stronger ability returns.

This lesson addresses a specific scenario: a person so ill that they can only perform head gestures (ima') for ruku' and sajdah, who then mid-prayer recovers enough to perform actual bowing and prostrating. The Hanafi school rules that the prayer must be restarted from the beginning. Why? Because head indication is legally considered the weakest form of prayer, and Islamic law does not allow building a higher form upon a lower one in this context. This ruling highlights a key principle about levels of ability in prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Initial Scenario
Recognize that the person begins praying with head indication (ima') due to inability to perform actual ruku' and sajdah.
Ima' is for extreme incapacity; even sitting is considered a higher level.
2
Recognize Change in Ability
During the prayer, the person recovers and becomes able to perform actual bowing and prostrating.
Ability must be genuine and stable; temporary relief does not trigger restart.
3
Apply the Ruling
The person must stop and restart the entire prayer from the beginning; the parts performed with ima' become invalid.
The ruling is 'wajib' according to the Hanafi school.
4
Understand the Rationale
Ima' is the weakest form; it cannot serve as a foundation for a stronger form, unlike sitting which is considered 'half-standing' and can be built upon.
There is no hadith evidence for building upon ima', so caution demands restarting.
5
Contrast with Sitting (Qa'dah)
Recall that a person who begins sitting and then can stand mid-prayer may continue from where they left off; sitting is a valid level that supports building up. Ima' is not.
This contrast clarifies why the ruling differs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فان صلى بعض صلوته بايماء ثم قدر على الركوع والسجود استأنف الصلوة
پس اگر بعض نماز اشارہ سے پڑھی پھر رکوع اور سجدہ پر قدرت ہو گئی تو نماز شروع سے پڑھے گا۔ اشارہ کرنا بہت ہی کمزور حالت ہے۔ اس پر اعلی کی بنا نہیں کر سکتے (۲) یہی وجہ ہے کہ لیٹنے والے یا اشارہ کرنے والے کی اقتدا میٹھنے والے یا کھڑے ہونے والے نہیں کر سکتے ہیں۔ کیونکہ ایک بہت اعلی حالت ہے اور دوسری بہت اونی حالت ہے۔ اس لئے اشارہ کر کے نماز پڑھ رہا تھا اور درمیان میں رکوع اور سجدہ پر قدرت ہوگئی تو اس پر بنا نہیں کرے گا بلکہ شروع سے نماز پڑھے گا (۳) اوپر کی حدیث سے ثابت ہوا کہ بیٹھنے پر کھڑے ہونے کو بنا آپ نے کیا ہے۔ لیکن اشارہ کرنے پر بنا کرنے کی حدیث نہیں ہے۔ اس لئے احتیاط کا تقاضا یہ ہے کہ اشارہ کرنے پر رکوع و سجدہ کرنے کو بنا نہ کیا جائے۔
If he prayed part of his prayer with indication, then he became able to bow and prostrate, he restarts the prayer.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This ruling contrasts with the previous ones. If a person was so ill that he could only perform head indications (ima') for both ruku' and sajdah, and during the prayer he recovers enough to perform actual ruku' and sajdah, he must restart the entire prayer. The reason is that head indication is a very weak form of prayer; it cannot serve as a foundation for the stronger form (actual movements). Unlike sitting (which is considered 'half-standing'), indication is at the lowest level and cannot be built upon. There is no hadith evidence permitting building upon indication, so out of precaution, one must start over. This also explains why a person praying sitting can lead a person praying standing, but a person indicating cannot lead either a sitting or standing person.

Key Terms
الايماء (al-īmā'): Head gestures for ruku' and sajdah when unable to perform actual movements.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Continue the prayer normally from where he left off
B) Restart the entire prayer from the beginning
C) Repeat only the rak'ahs prayed with indication
D) Complete the prayer with indication and not change

Key Takeaway
When ability to perform actual ruku' and sajdah returns during a prayer performed with head indication, the entire prayer must be restarted from the beginning.

Ruling on Qada for Prayers Missed Due to Fainting
Ruling on qada for fainting spells

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on qada for prayers missed due to fainting, apply the threshold of five prayers, and compare it with the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
Fainting removes taklif, but qada is required for 5 or fewer missed prayers; for more, it is waived.

This lesson addresses an important concession (rukhsah) in Hanafi fiqh: when a person misses prayers due to unconsciousness. The school distinguishes between short fainting spells (up to five prayers) and prolonged ones. Understanding this distinction helps you apply the principle that shari'ah obligation is lifted during unconsciousness, yet a limited obligation to make up missed prayers remains for short durations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling and Threshold
Note that the text states: five prayers or fewer must be made up; more than five are not. Memorize this number as the key distinction.
The number five corresponds to the typical number of fard prayers in a day and night (excluding witr).
2
Understand the Rationale (Ta'lil)
The commentary explains that fainting suspends the intellect, thus removing shari'ah address (taklif). However, for a short duration (≤5 prayers), qada is still required because the lapse is minor and can be compensated. For longer periods, full exemption applies.
This is analogous to sleep: a person who misses prayers while asleep must make them up, but prolonged unconsciousness is treated differently.
3
Compare with the Shafi'i Position
The Shafi'i school holds that all missed prayers due to unconsciousness, regardless of duration, must be made up. Identify the basis of this difference: Shafi'i do not make a distinction based on duration.
The Hanafi ruling is considered a rukhsah (concession) based on ease.
4
Apply the Ruling to Scenarios
Practice with examples: a person misses 3 prayers due to fainting → must make up. Misses 6 prayers → no qada required. Ensure you correctly count the number of prayers corresponding to the duration.
If the fainting lasts longer but the person only missed exactly 5 prayers? That counts as ≤5 and qada is needed. The threshold is the number of prayers missed, not the duration per se.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولمن اغمى عليه خمس صلوات او اقل قضاها وان كان اكثر من خمس صلوات لا يقضيها
جس پر پانچ نمازیں یا اس سے کم کی بیہوشی طاری ہوئی تو ان کو قضا کرے گا جب تندرست ہوگا۔ اور اگر فوت ہو گئی ہے بیہوشی کی وجہ سے پانچ نمازوں سے زیادہ تو قضا نہیں کرے گا۔ تشریح بیہوشی کی وجہ سے پانچ نماز یا اس سے کم قضا ہوئی ہو تو اس کو قضا کرے گا۔ اور اس سے زیادہ قضا ہو گئی ہو تو اس کو قضا نہیں کرے گا۔
معاف ہے۔
(1) بیہوشی طاری ہوئی تو عقل گویا کہ ختم ہو گئی اس لئے شریعت کا خطاب اس سے اٹھ گیا۔ لیکن ایک دن ایک رات سے کم بیہوشی رہی تو وہ
And whoever fainted, five prayers or less, he makes them up; and if it was more than five prayers, he does not make them up.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This ruling addresses qada (make-up) for missed prayers due to unconsciousness (ghashy). The Hanafi position: if the fainting lasted for a period during which five or fewer prayers were missed, those prayers must be made up upon recovery. If more than five prayers were missed (e.g., due to prolonged coma), then no qada is required. The Sharh explains that fainting removes the address of Shari'ah (taklif) because the intellect is suspended. However, for a short duration (five prayers or less), the missed prayers are considered a small lapse and must be compensated. Beyond that, the person is excused. The text ends abruptly with an incomplete phrase about 'one day and one night'. This likely means that if fainting lasts less than a day and night, it is considered the same as five prayers or less.

Key Terms
اغماء (ighmā'): Fainting or unconsciousness lasting a period during which prayers are missed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) Not make up any of them
D) Make up only the first prayer

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the obligation to make up prayers missed due to fainting is limited to five or fewer prayers; beyond that, no qada is required.

Building Upon Prayer When Recovering from Sitting to Standing
Ruling on building upon prayer when recovering from sitting to standing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on building upon prayer when recovering from sitting to standing, identify the condition that permits this, and cite the proof from the Sunnah.

Core Idea
A sick person who recovers during prayer continues standing from where he left off.

In the Hanafi school, a person who begins prayer sitting due to illness and then recovers during the prayer does not need to restart. Instead, he builds upon the prayer by standing and completing the remaining rak'ahs. This ruling is based on a prophetic practice and the principle that sitting is considered half of standing. Let's examine the precise conditions and evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognise that the ruling concerns a person who was compelled to pray sitting due to illness, then recovers mid-prayer. The ruling allows him to rise and continue the same prayer without starting over.
The act of building upon the prayer (bina') is not a separate obligation but a permission.
2
Locate the condition for the ruling
The condition is that the initial sitting posture was necessitated by genuine illness (marad). If one voluntarily sits without excuse, the ruling does not apply.
The illness must be a valid Shar'i excuse.
3
Examine the proof (dalil)
The proof is taken from the practice of the Prophet (peace be upon him) as narrated by 'Aisha (Muslim, no. 731); he began prayer sitting and then stood up and continued. Although this was for nafl, it indicates that building upon a different posture is permissible, and analogy (qiyas) extends it to fard prayers for those with illness.
The hadith is ahad but its indication (dalala) is considered definitive by the Hanafis.
4
Consider the implications
This ruling removes hardship; the prayer remains valid despite the posture change. Contrast with other schools that may require restarting.
The principle of 'sitting is half of standing' supports continuity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصلوة
(8) ومن صلى قاعدا يركع ويسجد لمرض ثم صح بنى على صلوته قائما
Book of Prayer. (8) And whoever prayed sitting, bowing and prostrating due to illness, then recovered, he builds upon his prayer standing.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This is a ruling from the Kitab al-Salah. The author states that if a person was unable to stand due to illness and prayed sitting while performing ruku' and sujud normally, and then recovers during the prayer, he should continue his prayer standing, building upon what he has already performed. He does not need to restart. The Urdu commentary explains that sitting is considered half of standing, as evidenced by the fact that a standing person can follow a sitting imam but not a lying one. A hadith from 'Aisha (Muslim, no. 731) is cited where the Prophet (peace be upon him) began prayer sitting, then stood up and continued, building upon the former. Although this hadith pertains to nafl prayers, it indicates that building upon a different posture is permissible in cases of illness for fard prayers as well. Thus, the ruling is that recovery from illness allows one to transition from sitting to standing without requiring a new start.

Key Terms
بنی على صلوته (bana 'ala salatihi): To continue the same prayer from the point left off, without restarting, despite a change in posture.
قاعدا (qa'idan): Sitting posture for prayer when unable to stand due to illness.

Key Takeaway

Restarting Prayer When Recovering from Gesture-Based Prayer
Ruling on restarting prayer when recovering from ima' to actual ruku' and sujud.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for when a person recovers from gesturing (ima') to actual ruku' and sujud during the same prayer, and explain the qiyas basis for requiring restart.

Core Idea
If you recover from gestures mid-prayer, you must restart — a lower state cannot support a higher one.

Imagine you are so ill that you pray by nodding your head. Midway, you regain enough strength to actually bow and prostrate. Does your prayer continue intact, or must you begin again? The Hanafi school gives a clear answer: restart. This lesson unpacks the ruling and its logical foundation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the case and the ruling
Read the Arabic text: 'If he prayed some of his prayer with gestures (ima'), then became able to bow and prostrate, he restarts the prayer.' Note the action (gesturing) and the recovery.
The ruling is explicit: isti'naf al-salah — restart the prayer.
2
Understand the rationale: qiyas on following an imam
The text compares this to the rule that a person who gestures cannot be followed by someone who can sit or stand. Apply this: if a lower state (gestures) cannot be followed by a higher state in congregation, then in an individual prayer, one cannot switch from gestures to actual movements.
The illah is the vast difference in level between gesture and actual ruku'/sujud.
3
Contrast with the sitting-to-standing case
Earlier rulings allow building from sitting to standing because of a hadith. For gestures, there is no such evidence, and the disparity is greater.
This distinction shows the importance of textual evidence (dalil naqli) over pure analogy.
4
Apply the ruling in practice
If a patient starts prayer with gestures and recovers before the end, they must break off and restart the entire prayer properly.
This preserves the order and harmony of the prayer postures.

Source Text

Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This ruling distinguishes between different levels of incapacity. If a person was so weak that he could only perform gestures (ima') for ruku' and sujud, and then during the prayer recovers the ability to actually bow and prostrate, he must restart the prayer from the beginning. The Urdu commentary explains that gestures indicate a very weak state, and one cannot build a higher posture upon a lower one. This is analogous to the rule that a standing or sitting person cannot follow a person praying with gestures (as imam). There is no hadith evidence to allow building in this case, unlike the previous ruling where a hadith supports building from sitting to standing. Therefore, for caution, the prayer must be restarted.

Qada for Prayers Missed Due to Fainting
Ruling on making up missed prayers when a person faints for up to five or more prayers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on making up prayers missed due to fainting and apply the threshold of five prayers.

Core Idea
Only up to five prayers missed due to fainting require qada; prolonged unconsciousness lifts the obligation entirely.

This lesson addresses the unique ruling on qada (make-up) for prayers missed due to fainting (ighma'). Unlike sleep, where all missed prayers must be made up, fainting can remove legal responsibility. The Hanafi school sets a clear boundary: if a person misses five prayers or less, they must make them up; if more, they are excused. This ruling is based on the practice of the Companion Ibn Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) and balances the principle of obligation with the removal of hardship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is being ruled? Making up missed prayers after fainting. The hukm: wajib for up to five prayers, not required for more.
The ruling is conditional on the number of missed prayers.
2
Understand the basis: fainting vs sleep
Fainting removes intellect, but short faints (≤5 prayers) resemble sleep; longer faints lift responsibility.
The key distinction is the duration of unconsciousness.
3
Locate the dalil (evidence)
The practice of Ibn Umar: he did not make up prayers when he fainted for a day and night or longer. This is a precedent (qawl sahabi) used as evidence.
The dalil is zanni al-thubut and zanni al-dalalah.
4
Apply the threshold
Count the missed prayers: if total ≤ 5, qada is wajib; if >5, no qada.
Five prayers correspond to approximately one day and night.
5
Note the cross-madhhab difference
Other schools may require qada for all prayers, but Hanafi relies on this specific precedent to avoid hardship.
The Hanafi position is considered mukhtalaf fih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(10) ولمن اغمى عليه خمس صلوات فما دونها قضاها اذا صح وان فاتته بالاغماء اكثر من ذلك لم يقض
(10) And whoever faints, five prayers or less, he makes them up when he recovers; if more than that are missed due to fainting, he does not make them up.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This ruling deals with qada (making up) of prayers missed due to fainting (ighma'). The text specifies that if a person remains unconscious for a period causing him to miss up to five prayers (i.e., less than about 24 hours, since five prayers roughly correspond to a day and night), he must make them up upon recovering consciousness. However, if he misses more than five prayers due to prolonged unconsciousness, he is not required to make them up. The Urdu commentary explains that fainting removes legal responsibility (khitab) because the person loses intellect. But if the unconsciousness lasts less than a day and night, it is akin to sleep, so qada is required. If it exceeds that, the person is excused to avoid hardship (haraj). This is supported by athar (traditions) from Ibn Umar: once he fainted for a day and night and did not make up missed prayers, and another time for a month and did not make up, but prayed the prayer of the day he regained consciousness. Thus, the maximum for qada is five prayers (a day and night).

Key Terms
اغمى عليه (ughmiya 'alayhi): A state of unconsciousness (fainting) that removes legal capacity.
قضاها (qadaha): To perform the missed prayer as qada (make-up) after recovery.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person faints for three days, how many prayers must he make up upon recovery according to the Hanafi school?
A) All 15 prayers
B) None
C) Only the first five prayers
D) Only the first day's prayers

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school exempts from qada any prayers missed due to fainting beyond five, based on the precedent of Ibn Umar, to remove hardship.
